A LARGE BONUS.
The Company has recently forwarded to the holder of policy No. 278,127 a cheque for
£1,099 13s. 6d. in payment of the cash of the Bonus for 1891, the
Policy being for 10,000, and issued in 1886, on the five year distribution plan. This
return is equal to an annual cash bonus of £2 4s. for each £lOO insured. Many of
these po icies are reaching the bonus period with results very gratifying to the Insured.
The total payments to Policy-holders to December, 1890, amounted to £63,469,822,
of which upwards of £17,400,000 were bonus payments —more than twice tf& amount of
Bonuses paid by any other Company.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 7, 1891.
Can England Hold India?
It is not for soldiers alone that war finds
employment. With the discharge of the
first gun or the appearance of the first
cavalry scout, the world’s scribblers at once
seek to turn an honest penny by appearing
as war-correspondents sufficiently near the
scene of action to be reasonably safe, or as
novelists to stay at home and manufacture
thrilling tales of love and slaughter while the
opportunity offers—that is, while men's
minds are swayed by the sound of strife. It
was so during the Crimean struggle, during
the Indian mutiny, and, later still, during
the murderous contest between the Northern
Democracy, determined on the freedom that
could only he preserved by solidarity, and
the Southern Oligarchy prepared for selfish
ends to drive a wedge into the Union. It was
in a noyelette touching on some episodes in
the Indian uprising, which so nearly destroyed
in a day the work of a century, that a literary
hack in the neighbourhood of Ratcliffe High-
way wrote of Hindostan as “An Empire
won by the sword, but which the Bible alone
can retain.” At that time the Sepoys in
squads were being blown away from guns.
It is therefore but fair to the British authori-
ties of the time to say that they recognised
the expediency of not depending solely on
the Word of God. The Bible is admirable
in many ways ; but its persuasive power is
in no way diminished by alliance with a
field-piece.
* * •
It may he said without irreverence that
the part hitherto played by the Jewish
scriptures in governing the great Asiatic
dependency has been insignificant. It will
probably remain so. Nor is there much
evidence to show that, even if the bulk of
the inhabitants accepted the European
Trinity instead of their own gods, any great
benefit would result, so far, at least, as this
world is concerned. European nations
have enjoyed in some instances the
blessings of the true faith for 15 or 16
centuries with the result that at the present
moment millions of young men go to bed
every night with rifles and ammunition
within easy reach. Hence, in order to hold
India there appears at present to be no
better method than the primitive one of
braining those who desire to take it. In a
word, the country was not only won by the
sword, but will have to be retained, if
retained at all, by the same weapon. The
case is but a modem illustration of the well-
known dictum, to place rather more trust in
powder than in Providence.
# * #
At the present time considerable atten-
tion is focussed on the broad tracts of
country drained by the Indus and ex-
tending from the foot of the Himalayas to
the sea under the Northern Tropic. It is
probably in the neighbourhood of this great
Punjaub stream that India will he won or
lost. For many years past the conquerors
have kept a jealous eye on this north-western
border-land, which is occupied by the Sikhs,
the fiercest of warriors, and which is being
daily approached by the Russians. So long
as Britain holds the sea, danger can only
come from the north-west, except, as in 1857,
when the trouble is entirely internal. Within
the last week or two, brief telegrams of
ominous import have came from this region,
or rather from the region just to the north,
whore the Himalayas, having run themselves
out, degenerate into the Hindoo Koosh,
or form little knots of mountainous outlying
sentries, as if to guard the upper angle of
the Punjaub. It is about here that, after
some centuries of gradual advance, the Cos-
sack has at last run into the Afghan-
impinged upon him, so to say—and there
they stand at this moment biting thumbs at
eacli other with every prospect of arbitrating
with the bayonet before long. In the mean-
time, two spectators, one from the extreme
east, the other from the far west of the old
continent —one with a pig-tail, the other
with spats—stand by to show fair play or to
take a hand in the dispute, according to cir-
cumstances. Nobody supposes the Muscovite
wants the barren Afghan rocks or would risk
a Jewish soldier for a right-of-way through
one of the valleys, except as a highway to
the fruitful plains further south.
* * *
To state the case properly we must go
hack. Considerably over a hundred years
ago a Frenchman, who happened to he in
India, thought that with good fighting,
good allies, and good luck, an Empire might
be collected from the ruins of the Mogul
power and held together by a little pluck.
He would have done it, too, but for the
inevitable Briton who, with the modesty of
his countrymen, observed, “If empires are
to be had for the asking, I don’t see why
I should remain silent.” So Robert Clive
cut in. He did more—he cut the Gaul out
and, with a creditable absence of favoritism
—combined with a rare aptitude for imitat-
ing signatures—he served everybody else
the same way. France might have had the
country by sending out a few regiments of
real soldiers and not sending the scum of the
Faubourgs, hut the reigning Bourbon of the
day was more interested in mistresses than
in Moguls, so India slipped through M.
Crapaud’s fingers and fell into J. Bull’s
arms where she has remained ever since.
To do Johnny B. justice, he has stuck to her
with perseverance, probably for her dowry,
for over a century. But the question is now
cropping up on all sides—can he stick to her
much longer ?
* # »
For a long time past the Russian has been
moving south and south-west. They were
the only ways open to him. The north was
blocked by ice ; the east by China, and the
west by Europe. Again, the nations he had
to over-run towards the south were nomadic,
disunited and weak. Then there was the
further object of being within striking
distance of the British, so that future diplo-
matic notes between the two empires might
be written in full view of the fact that
100,000 good Russian troops, accompanied
by more than double the number of Central
Asian canaille, were not so far from the
Indus. For a long time, British statesmen
and writers made merry at the idea of Russia’s
being able to move large bodies of men across
the immense stretch of inhospitable country
between the Caspian and Cabul. Even a
few years ago Lord Salisbury told the
London alarmists to take down their maps
and reckon the spaces to be traversed.
To-day, when telegrams come from England
reporting scuffles between Muscovites and
Afghans at the head water's of the Indus, it
is pretty certain that the distance has been
negotiated somehow. It now remains to be
seen what means the Bi'itish have of stem-
ming this constantly-advancing wave, of
keeping it from breaking on the Punjaub
when the next international complications
give the signal, and of sweeping Lower
India, as many other conquering waves have
swept it from the same quarter.
# # *
The white force in India is about 70,000
men fit, probably, to meet any equal number
in the world. Unfortunately, these cannot
be concentrated in the north-west without
risking a rebellion all through the central and
southern parts of the peninsula. For there
is no disguising the fact —it leaks out in the
bazaars, in the native press, in the semi-
independent States, and in a thousand
different ways —that throughout the Great
Dependency John Bull is no favourite.
The British can conquer, but they cannot
be compared with the Russians for ingratiat-
ing themselves with native races. Whether
through coldness or pride, formality or
arrogance, they offend native susceptibilities
at every turn. It might be thought that,
with a population of 250,000,000 to fallback
upon for recruits, armies could he raised
that would not only stem any Russian
advance hut sweep it hack through the
lately-acquired Khanates almost to the
Caspian. Such might be the case if the
armies could be depended on. It is here the
difficulty comes in. Not only will white
troops have to be left behind when a move-
ment is made north-west, but, for various
reasons, it will be impossible to place in the
front line any but a few native corps d’elite
chosen not alone for their superior discipline
but for the loyalty of the nations from which
they have been selected. For it should not be
forgotten that India, like Europe, is a con
geries of nations, most of them bitterly hos-
tile to each other, but, unlike Europe, held
together in a Roman peace by the mastery of
a distant and, in their eyes, unscrupulous
Power. In short, to hold India, England
will be forced to depend much more upon
the number of white troops she can place in
line than ever she has had to do since Clive
conquered at Plassy. A check at the open-
ing of the ball would mean tumult behind
and the assembling of hordes of semi-savages
in front eager to take a hand in shaking the
Pagoda-tree.
# * #
From what has been said, it follows that,
if India is to be held, it will only he by the
power of Britain to pour in a few hundred
thousand white troops when the necessity
arrives. This can only be done by universal
military service at home. But it is almost
certain that the people of the United
Kingdom would sooner see Hindostan drift
away from the Empire than submit to
such a measure. Ever since Cromwell and
his Major-Generals showed the British people
what military rule was, the people have dis-
played an invincible repugnance to universal
liability to serve. They did not resort to it
for the purpose of holding America, nor
during the long, doubtful struggle against
the power of Napoleon. True it is that
sturdy felons wore released from gaol in
batches on the condition of serving against
Washington in one hemisphere or against
Soult in another. But, for reasons which
it would he tedious to give in detail—some
of them connected with the class or caste
distinctions still so potent in British society
—the repugnance to general conscription is
still so deep-seated that not even the threat
of a foreign landing would overcome it. The
idea of Lord Tomnoddy carrying a musket
beside Tommy Atkins and rubbing shoulders
with the latter in camp and public-house is
not to he entertained. Yet, the time is at
hand, may be upon us any day, when, with-
out this universal liability to defend the
Empire at all points, Great Britain will be
forced back into the domain in which she
has hitherto been supreme— the domain of
the four seas.
Manufacturing Australian Imperialism.
Parson Fitchett, late editor of the Melbourne
Daily Telegraph, declares, per medium of the
Pall Mall Gazette, that “Australian senti-
ment is even more Imperialist than public
opinion in Great Britain itself.” He describes
Australians as being “aggressively proud of
the Empire and of the Flag,” and laughs to
scorn the very supposition of the existence of
any such thing as an Australian National senti-
ment of Independence. He describes Austra-
lians thus:—
They are, . . . even absurdly jealous of their
partnership in the Empire, and to describe them as being
eager to “ cut the painter” is the most absurd travesty
of facts.
Perhaps so. Perhaps Premier Debus, of New
South Wales, lias never declared that an Inde-
pendent Republic is Australia’s inevitable
destiny. Perhaps, ex-Premiers MTlwraith
and More head, of Queensland, have never
advocated Australian separation from England.
Perhaps ex-Premier O’Loghlen, of Victoria,
and other prominent politicians in Fitchett’s
own recent colony, have never talked of
“ cutting the painter.” Perhaps the Imperial
Institute is not the laughing-stock of the
colonies. Perhaps every itinerant Imperial
Federation lecturer has not been a frost in Aus-
tralia, has not been refused a hearing, and has
not been sat upon even by such conservative
journals as the Sydney Morning Herald and
the Sydney Daily Telegraph. Fitchett flies
to the one stock act of insanity that can give
any colour to his false presentment of Australian
aspirations :
It was the Imperial strain in Australian blood which in-
spired the Soudan contingent of New South Wales. The
spectacle of what seemed disaster to the Empire simply
set Australian blood on fire.
If so, it is the Australian democratic strain in
this people’s blood that makes a repetition of
that freak impossible. We agree with Mr.
Fitchett that—
The reflex effect of the despatch of the Sydney contin-
gent to the Soudan upon the Australian colonies them-
selves was very marked.
The Soudan contingent died of popular ridicule.
Its only memory now is its patron goat. Even
its pewter medals are forgotten. It is chiefly
remembered as a wild joke which provided a
well-paid picnic for a number of unemployed
ex-soldiers ; when it is seriously recollected, it
is as a hideous lampoon of impossible future
tragic absurdities. Parson Fitchett must be
insanely oblivions to the pulsations of Austra-
lian life wdien he rashly writes :
I am confident that if to-day any great disaster smote,
or threatened to smite, the Empire ; if an English fleet
had been destroyed in the Channel, or a new mutiny
broke out in India, the colonies would leap to the side of
the mother land, and stand ready to pour out their
treasure and spill their blood in its cause.
They might and they might not. The majority
of the chances are that Australian colonists
would promptly look after themselves and their
home belongings, if the outlook became criti-
cally warlike. Even if it were admitted for the
sake of argument —and it would be an un-
warranted admission to make—that a majority
of Australians place Imperial before Colonial
interests—are, in fact, Britishers first and
Australians a bad second, The Bulletin would
still confidently predict that never again will
any Australian Government dare to propose
the expenditure of a single penny of public
money for foreign warlike purposes. If the
colonies ever do “leap to the side of the
mother land,” the feat will only be performed
by volunteers in their private capacities —
buccaneering mercenaries who probably would,
for prospect of loot, as readily tender their
swords to the Emperor of China to suppress a
rebellion against the Manchurian dynasty.
Parson Fitchett might as well attempt to
demonstrate the non-existence of the moon,
because it is invisible at certain periods, as to
deny the vitality of a growing sentiment of
Republicanism in Australia. His perplexity of
argument is irritably evidenced by his falling
back upon that gorgeous historian, Froude ;
“ Any speaker in Australia,” Mr. Froude wrote in his
“ Oceana,” “ who advocated Separation at a public meet-
ing would be hooted down,” and that is as true to-day as
when Mr. Froude penned the words. “ The Australians,”
he added, “are proud of belonging to a nationality on
whose Hag the sun never sets ; and Separation, if it comes,
will be no act of theirs.” How shrewdly Mr. Froude
interpreted Australian sentiment, a thousand facts prove.
A few years ago, for example, a cluster of Irish members
of Parliament in Victoria signed what was known as the
“ Grattan Address.” The address was a bit of very mild
Home Rule literature, and yet the act of signing it cost
every member of this group, save one, his seat when the
next general election arrived !
Froudesquerio and Fitchettism ! Not a month
goes by but Separation is publicly advocated
somewhere in Australia, and is cheered to the
echo. The fate of the signatories to the
“ Grattan Address” was itself a lively proof
of the vitality of Australian Nationalism. It
was not a locally political issue; it was an
ebullition of religious sectarianism. It roused the
animus of professional Protestantism, the
catspaw's of which were beguiled into
a voting crusade against it. But it
further enlisted the sympathies of that
large Australian majority which declines
to allow sectarian strife to be imported
from old-world countries, and thrown into the
midst of this democracy as a bone for venomous
and bloody wranglings. Young Australia has
declared a rule absolute for the divorce of the
State from religion. Complete credal freedom
is one of the inviolable pillars of a democratic
constitution. No political crisis in Australia
has ever yet turned upon the issue, Imperialism
v. Republicanism, but the harvest is ripening for
that decisive occasion. Australia’s final destiny
never has been nor ever can be in doubt. It
was even predicted half-a-century ago by the
late Dr. Lang, a statue to whom was erected
the other day amid the loud acclaim
of our people. The apathy with which the
Imperialist fireworks of the late Federation
Convention have been received throughout Aus-
tralia is but one sign among all the evidences of
the time. In spite of strenuous artificial boom-
ing by plotting politicians, in spite of the
tinsel glamour and pinchbeck ostentation en-
wrapped around the whole posing, the idea of
“Federation under the Crown” has never
stirred the popular pulse nor awakened one
real echo of public approval. Sir George Grey
was the only Conventionist whose utterances
vibrated to Australia’s vital centres; and his
magic was the music of National Independence.
So palpably has the pretence of the Convention
palled that Australian Government after Aus-
tralian Government, forgetting each its past
Imperialist heroics, has weariedly let the lifeless
abortion drop until a more convenient season
presents itself for resuscitating it. That more
convenient season will never arrive. The
Federation to which Australia will pledge itself
unreservedly will be of unforced free birth —the
offspring of self-reliance and disloyalty to the
Crown. Fitchett’s Australian Imperialists are
phantoms, manufactured by a diseased fancy,
with the exception of a few nonentities whose
petty souls can be purchased at any time witli
a greasy smile and a half-inch of silk ribbon.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Justice and Clothes.
A few days ago Petek Howe, M.P., and
James Miller, both of the lately gone bung
Australian Mercantile Loan and Guarantee
Company, were duly sentenced by Judge lnnes
to seven years gaol for undue fooling with the
assets of that lost corporation. In the light of
the evidence the sentence seems to meet the
case admirably ; and if sundry other directors,
representing various companies that are already
burst, and various others that ought to be
burst, should presently go the same road, the
results will probably prove beneficial to the
public at large. The average director doesn’t
get into gaol often enough. In a large
number of cases he is an individual with no
qualification ; he begins by holding the smallest
number of shares which will qualify him for a
seat on the board, and when he gets his seat,
he sells, but does not transfer them, and there-
after he draw's his fees under false pretences,
and plays with other people’s money joyously,
having none of his own at stake. No director
that we ever heard of has got into gaol for this,
though several hundred most assuredly ought
to get there. Then he votes himself handsome
overdrafts and loans on no visible security, and
this is as strictly wholesale stealing as if he
took his overdraft out of the “bank” by
means of a crowbar and a jemmy. Very few'
directors get into gaol for this either—in fact, at
the present moment we can’t recollect one. Also,
he votes for the purchase of his own land by the
company, and values the land himself, whereby
it occasionally happens that a hunk of rock in
the back-blocks, worth something like £lO on a
liberal estimate, is passed oft on the share-
holders for £5OO or £lOOO. It is very seldom
indeed that a director gets locked up for this
either, but Messrs. Howe and Miller did the
business with such phenomenal clumsiness that
they just managed to crowd themselves safely
into quod. In a general way a fraudulent
director has almost to burst his way Into a gaol
if he wants to get there, and the success of
Peter Howe and his mate in getting themselves
put away is a surprising circumstance. The
evidence against them was absolutely conclusive,
but it took the most vigorous and vociferous
efforts on the part of Judge Innes to secure a
conviction, and it was only by dint of almost
unjustifiable pressure that the verdict of
“ guilty ” was extracted. It would have been
a lasting disgrace had the verdict not been ob-
tained, but yet it would really appear that if
the Judge had been even a shade less urgent, the
result would have been a failure. The average
juryman, after all, has a superstitious reverence
for the prisoner in the shiny black coat. Ho
returns his verdict cheerfully, and on the most
insufficient evidence, to hang or imprison the
individual in a tattered suit, but in nine cases
out of ten he will acquit the fat, well-dressed
swindler without the slightest hesitation.
The only swindler who doesn’t get a show under
present conditions, is the one who hasn’t stolen
enough to enable him to buy good clothes with


the proceeds. Therefore, if justice is to he a
square and honest thing, and all men are to
have the same chance, every prisoner who is
tried on a serious charge should be supplied
with a decent suit, so that he may only be
hanged for his crime instead of being hanged
for the tears in his coat. The clothes ought to
be reasonably up to date and suitable to his
complexion, and he should be allowed to carry
a shiny bell-topper in his hand. The disused
bell-topper of Mr. Howe, duly ironed up for the
occasion by the Government laundryman,
might, for instance, serve a long line of
prisoners, and save many of them from receiv-
ing unjust sentences, and as the accused would
simply carry it in his hand the question of lit
is a matter of no importance. Also, each
prisoner ought to be shaved and have Ids hair
nicely done in a George-street manner -and
even then the results would probably be un-
satisfactory. For when two men came up for
trial in two equally elegant black coats the
jury would convict the man who wore the
Government garment, and would promptly
acquit the fellow who had paid for his own suit
out of his plunder.
Faddle for the Farmers.
J. B. Patterson, the able-bodied North
Country windbag, who travels Victoria in the
interests of Her Gracious Majesty and the
British Lion, has been loading up the worthy
agriculturists of Castlemaine and district with
reasons why the doctrine of One-man-one-vote
is a vain thing. Victoria, according to the
inspired Jingo, is a “propertied democracy,”
and the result of restricting each man to a vote
for his manhood would be the disfranchisement
of property and consequently the “ disfranchise-
ment of the democracy.” Involved fiddledeedee
like this may suffice to stagger a roomfull of
Ducolics after a heavy feed, but it won’t pass
muster with any plain, unbiassed person who
considers it on an empty stomach. In the first
place, Victoria isn’t a propertied democracy,
inasmuch as a large number of the adult male
inhabitants are too young, too poorly paid, or
too often out of work to have obtained what
the representative, fat-headed Tory calls a
“stake in the country.” This, without men-
tioning the hard-up class, consisting of a few
constitutional loafers and many who are
knocked out through ill-luck, lack of self-
confidence, or a long bout of sickness, all
of whom would be glad to possess the
tail-end of a muttor shop, or a chunk
of cold meat in the country. In the
second place, there couldn’t exist a democracy
which founded its right to vote on a property
basis, because no kind of democrat except the
J.B.P. brand desires to puff up to the poll in
the capacity of a terrace of houses, a well-
stocked station, a prosperous commercial under-
taking, or a quantity of corner allotments. He
simply wishes to vote as a man, one man—not
as several investments in land which ought to
belong to the State. In the third place, the
English language doesn’t admit of property
being disfranchised, and even if that arrange-
ment were sanctioned by the rules of grammar
it couldn’t come to pass, because a property-
owner is put upon the voters’ roll as such, and,
strictly speaking, votes by virtue of his “ stake
in the country.” Likewise, the fact of his stake
being divided into several portions doesn’t
touch the main point that one man can only
have one stake, anyway. Finally, the mind of
Patterson must endeavour to grasp the almighty
truth that the disfranchisement .of a democracy
is the wildest political impossibility ever con-
ceived by any shallow sophist at a farmers’
luncheon. Meanwhile, however, it appears
that the apostle of proputty looks scornfully
upon the One-man-one-vote agitation because it
isn’t calculated to put “a chicken in the pot”
of every hungry inhabitant of Victoria. Still,
it will undoubtedly go a little way in that
direction, and if the immediate result is to put
noisy old roosters like Patterson in the pot
democracy will have much to be thankful for.
.
A more extraordinary political spectacle than
that exhibited by the Victorian Government
over Munro’s Franchise Bill would be difficult
to conceive. Premier Munro, on behalf of the
Government, accepted certain amendments in
Committee of the Assembly, and a measure vir-
tually affirming the principle of One-man-one-
vote was passed on to the Legislative Council.
Ihe leader of the Government there, Sir
Frederick Sargood, moved the second reading

Ihf, Bulletin fails to see what the Labour
party has to gain by opposing the election of
Edmund Barton for East Sydney. It is true
that the 1 )ibbs-Barton alliance is a grotesque
anomaly if viewed from the standpoint of their
disagreement concerning Australian Federation,
but it is equally true that Australian Federa-
tion, as formulated by the recent Convention,
has dropped out of the political running. The
Convention Bill is as dead as an antediluvian
fossil. It is most improbable that Australian
I' education can be artificially forced in any
political hothouse; it must be a natural growth,
or the sudden countermove to a threatening
danger. At any rate, the Convention Bill forms
no part of the Hibbs policy, except as a
measure to be mutilated with a free hand, and
reconstructed in a national mould. It is becom-
ing more and more apparent that the old Federal
Council of Australia, despised by Parkes
from motives of ignored vanity, was a whole-
some and natural incubator of Australian
union, which might have been accomplished by
easy stages of necessary federal legislation,
until, by the creation of courts of common
jurisdiction, an Australian Court of Final
Appeal, and even a Customs Union, the final
step of complete partnership would have been
a very ordinary process of development. A
suddenly - forced statutory combination of
antagonistic elements was bound, like a chemi-
cal experiment, to end in either a disintegrating
fizzle or a dangerous explosion. So far, Australia
has experienced the fizzle. Apart from Federa-
tion disagreements, however—and these do not
really affect the present political situation in
New South Wales— Hibbs and Barton are
harmoniously mated in points of policy, and
the cue of the Labour party should be to extract
as much party benefit as possible from the
materials immediately available. Until Labour
is presented with the excalibur of One-man-
one-vote, it cannot hope to hew down for itself
all the obstacles that stand between present
social and economic conditions and the achieve-
ment of needed labour-reforms. The united
power of Labour should be concentrated first
on gaining the electoral key which alone can
unlock to Labour representatives the Parlia-
ment and the Government of the country.
Under date 10th September, James H. Kelly
writes us from San Francisco :
Editor Bulletin, — Sir. — Allow me space to publish
some facts which may interest your readers. lam a
labouring man, lived 18 years in Australia and Maoriland,
and worked principally in mines and on railroads. Six-
teen months ago 1 took passage from Sydney to San
tlancisco. It is for the benefit of my co-workers in
Australia that I now summarise my 15 months’ American
experiences, from a working-man's point of view. While
in the States I have worked on railroads and other works
affording the best market for my labour. I have travelled
through Washington, Idaho, and Colorado, three of the
best States for the miner, railroader, or woodsman, they
being new and well back from the crowded East which
means more pay and less competition in the labour-
market. All labourers and three-fourths of- the miners
here uork ten hours per day. The Southern Pacific
R. R. Co. work their men 11 hours daily, winter and
summer, in nearly all cases. The company or contractor
board their men, the latter finding their own bedding.
Labourers’ wages per day run from one dollar and sixty
cents to two dollars per day in this western country—
very much less in the eastern States. Miners’ wages
from 2J dollars (10s.) to 3)- (145.) ; labourers’ board
per week costs from 5 to 5J dollars ; miners’ board,
fi to 7 dollars. Pay-day occurs every month, the
company or contractor keeping back one month’s pay
which means that after working two months the work-
man receives one month’s pay. This combination also
runs a store stocked with boots, tobacco, candles, soap,&c.,
vended at an advance of from 70 to 120 per cent on town
prices; then again, if a man quit work before pay-day
his time-cheque is discounted from 15 to 25 per cent.
This is the general system, hut I must say it is not
according to law. This country is over-stocked with all
kinds of European and Asiatic labour. The Chinaman is
an important factor here in the building and maintaining
of railroads. I have seen them in British Columbia
making mortar and going right info the building busi-
ness. Altogether for a labouring man this place is, in my
opinion, a long way behind Australia, and 1 would
strongly advise miners and labourers to stay by the
country whose workmen’s motto is, “ eight hours for
manly labour, eight hours for balmy sleep, eight hours
for recreation, and our triple eight hours we keep.” 1
am informed by reliable and sterling men that the rail-
road repairers in the Eastern States and other such
workers’ pay per day, is from 90 to 135 cents-in English
money, 3s. 9d. to ss. (id. I am going back to Australia
next March.—Yours truly, James 11. Kelly, San Fran-
cisco, 10th Sept. 1891.

One gratifying result of the protective tariff
of Maoriland is the healthy stimulus given to
our woollen manufactures which, under Tory
misrule, were for long enough in a languishing
state,” says the Southlander. Little facts
like these constantly cropping up completely
extinguish all the dismal Freetrade prophecies
indulged in by the Sydney press when dealing
with Hibbs’ Protectionist manifesto. It is
curious, too, to note that Protectionist countries
are so enamoured of the ruin they experience
that they persist in wading more deeply into it.
England is very wroth "with America, for
instance, concerning its M'Kinley tariff, and
its Freetrade Press predicts immediate de-
struction of all American prosperity, but
Yankeeland deliberately prefers to rush to
destitution in its own fashion rather than
to live on imported British manufactures.
Similarly, Victoria is so disgusted with Protec-
tion that it is continually asking for more.
Again, whenever the London Times praised any
Imperialistic move of the arch-shuffler Parkes,
the Sydney dailies echoed the wise sweetness
of its enlightened opinions, and effervesced
with admiration at its intelligent grasp of Aus-
tralian affairs ; but now that the Times has
found occasion to praise Hibbs for attempting
to place New South Wales on a self-reliant
footing of independent industry, words fail such
journals—notably the H.T. t > express their
contempt for the dense ignorance of Australian
questions generally exhibited by London editors
and political economists. The mere fact that
the Times can speak favourably of protective
duties under any circumstances whatever is a
triumphant indication of the progress of pro-
tectionist principles in an unexpected quarter.
The Sydney Echo libels Premier Dices in this
fashion:—
Probably we have never had a Prime Minister who was
lesx under the control of capitalists and financiers than
Sir Henry Parses has been.
The Bulletin is obliged to the Echo for the
admission that Park es is under the control of
capitalists and financiers—look at his record !
for a period as long as the Jews were under the
control of Moses. Then-
lie does not understand finance.
This is just what Parkes does understand thor-
oughly. He is quite a master at raising a
loan.
And it has not been the habitof his life to consort much
with those who made public finance their study.
The members of his last two administrations
could tell a different tale if they would. But
the records of the Insolvency Court are
sufficient to satisfy the country that Parkes
has held a political retainer from the Calico-
Jimmy party from the day he turned his fiscal
coat.

The following ludicrously-eloquent poem in
praise of England, originally perpetrated by
one Henley in the National Observer (i.e.,
the old Scots Observer, in which Rudyard
Kipling was first introduced to the British
public), is reprinted for one sufficient reason.
It is the cry of J. Bull, the most self-
sufficient fat man who ever encumbered
creation. It is a frank acknowledgment
by Bull that he the said Bull —is
the noblest, grandest, and most courageous
individual on earth, that he is going to last for
ever, that he is the recognised flower of
humanity, that the universe wouldn't be worth
anything if he wasn’t in it, and that the Lord
may take a spell, for John Bull will keep things
straight while He is away. It is a candid
admission made by himself that he is the only
person who is exempt from the operation of the
laws of nature, and that nothing apart from
him is worth a curse, and, in short, it is the
widest, largest and most capacious hunk of
imbecile and diseased vanity that we have yet
met:—-
We are the Choice of the Will: God, when he gave th®
word
That called us into line, set in our hand a sword ;
Set us a sword to wield, none else could lift and draw,
And bade us forth to the sound of the trumpet of the
Law.
Erst and west and north, wherever the battle grew,
As men to a feast we flocked, the work of the Will to do.
Bent upon vast beginnings, bidding anarchy cease
(Had we hacked it to the Pit, we had left it in a place of
peace !)
Marching, building, sailing, pillar of cloud or fire,
Sons of the Will, we fought the fight of the Will, our sire.
Road was never so rough that we left its purpose dark ;
Stark was ever the sea, but our shins were yet more
stark.
We tracked the winds of the world to the roots of their
very thrones,
The secret parts of the world were salted with our bones ;
Till now the name of names, England, the name of might,
Flames from the Austral bounds to the ends of the
northern night.
And the call of her morning drum goes in a girdle of
sound,
Like the voice of the sun in song, the great globe round
and round;
And the shadow of her flag, when it blabs to the mother-
breeze,
Floats from shore to shore of the universal seas ;
And the loneliest death is fair with a memory of her
flowers,
And the end of the road to Hell with the sense of her
dews and showers.
Who says that we shall pass, or the fame of us fade and
die,
While the living stars fulfil their round in the living sky?
For the sire lives in his sons, and they pay their father’s
debt,
And the Lion has left a whelp wherever his claw was set.
And the Lion in his whelps, his whelps that none shall
brave,
Is but less strong than Time and the all-devouring Grave !
The master-hairdressers of Sydney propose
that their journeymen be paid according to their
abilities—classification to be made on the report
of a board of examiners. Just think of it—a
board of barbers !
The N.S.W. Employers’ Union has ap-
pointed a parliamentary committee to nominate
candidates and fight parliamentary elections.
Property is now politically represented in all the
Australian by numerous powerful organisations
besides its strenuous and unflagging advocate,
the daily press. The Pastoralists’ Unions,
the Employers’ Unions, the Shipowners’ Asso-
ciations and the bogus “National” Associations
and “Patriotic Leagues,” are all allied and fede-
rated and re-federated and cross-federated—fede-
rated forwards, backwards, sideways, and in-and-
outjon the fifteen-two, fifteen-four, fifteen-six-and-
a-pair-eight principle. No two of these organisa-
tions are pulling different ways. They are all
ready, if need be, to sacrifice their own father at
the abattoirs to get their candidates into the
Legislature. On the other hand, the present
tendency of the Labour Leagues—industriously
encouraged by the daily press—is to split up each
into half-a-dozen sections.
In “ silvery - tongued ” Kelynack, not
merely the Methodist Church alone, but the
ecclesiastical bodies of Australia have lost their
one great natural orator. He was a man to whom
ever, an agnostic journalist or an Anglican Bishop
could listen with delight. His Centennial sermon
was, without exception, the most striking
oratorical effort delivered during that period of
gush, gorge, and guzzle, and his preroration,
which included Kendall’s lines, “One hundred
years ago,” could not have been soon forgotten by
those who heard it. This discourse, by the way,
was first delivered in Bathurst (N.S. W.) Wesleyan
Church, and, curiously enough, the editor of the
Bathurst Times, who was about as regular a
frequenter of chapels as most journalists, heard
it and reported it in full in his paper, so impressed
was he by its unforced eloquence. Dr. Kelynack
was intensely annoyed at the circumstance of
publication, as he had intended his delivery to be
merely an experiment for his own guidance in
repeating the sermon in Sydney. He complained
to the editor of the circumstance, and was barely
placated by the response, “Well, Doctor, we can’t
let the Sydneyites have the only magnificent
speech of the celebration.”
Concerning the Epidemic.
W hen yon catch the influenza you must dodder
into bed,
And wonder what’s the matter, with a hot and
aching head:
Nor heed such airy trifles as the shivers or a
cough—
But cuss the man who tells you, “you must try
and shake it off. ”
Your sense of smell will vanish, your ability to
taste
Will vainly try to battle with a horrid yellow
paste
Lhat sticks upon the tongue and makes the flavour
of your drink
Like a pint of bitter aloes in a quart of marking-
ink.
Then you get the collywobbles in a casual kind of
way—
It mostly comes upon you at the dawning of the
day ;
\ ou spurn the choicest vittles, and at broth you
cannot look,
And you really long for nothing but to spiflicate
the cook.
J t s fun, the influenza—such a comical disease,
There’s a world of quiet humour in its incidental
sneeze:
It hasn t much beginning, and it seldom seems to
end,
And it never kills a patient till he’s fairly on the
mend.
That nice old man, the doctor, too, is also very
quaint,
He s as good a joke, God bless him! as the
horrible complaint;
He mentions that “ you’ve got it,” and he wags
his addled head,
And the upshot of his wisdom is—you’d better
stop in bed.
If his talent’s transcendental he will ask to see
your tongue,
And pass some observations on your liver and
your lung,
But it’s past his comprehension, and there’s little
he can tell,
Except that when you’re better you’ll be feeling
pretty well.
>So catch the influenza, which is hanging round
your door,
Count the flies upon the ceiling for a week or
two, or more ;
And avoid all sorts of physic save the life-blood
of the toff
Who saunters in to tell you, “ you must try and
shake it off.”
Melbourne.
E.F.
A Maoriland man lias got an action against
a book-canvasser on an entirely new basis. He
had ordered the customary big book in a whole
swag of monthly parts, but afterwards repenting
of the volume he refused to accept delivery, and
threatened the canvasser with personal damage
and battery. Being a prudent man, that indi-
vidual came along silently one evening, and
delivered the publication by leaving it on the
front doorstep. The buyer didn’t know it was
there, but an hour afterwards lie rushed out to
look at a dog-fight, and fell over the literature,
and broke his leg. He is now suing the canvasser
for damages on the ground that the book on the
doorstep was the cause of his mishap, and as he
will probably get a jury of twelve infuriated book-
buyers to consider the case he has an alluring
prospect ahead.
Harry Stockdale, who recently took a
party of buffalo-shooters from Sydney to the
Northern Territory, is said to have gone as local
representative of an Adelaide syndicate which
recently leased a tract of country 40 x 60 miles
square, extending back from Port Essington, and
which it is proposed to stock in the sweet bye-
and-bye. Meanwhile Stockdale will explore and
likewise exploit the country in the hopes of
securing enough buffaloes and ponies to pay ex-
penses. What he will do with the ponies when
he gets them no man knows, but the buffalo-hides
bring at present about per lb., with unlimited
demand. For buggy-brake leathers nothing beats
buffalo-hide, as it wears for ever. The heads and
horns also bring good prices if well mounted.
Apropos, Stockdale will be bitterly disappointed
over the failure of his taxidermist to join the party
at Cooktown as arranged.
It is cabled from London that in “further
concentration tests carried out by Mr. Luhrig on
Victorian tailings, Bendigo tailings,, assaying only
some few pennyweights to the ton, concentrated
sozs. to the ton at a cost of Od. per ton. St.
Arnaud tailings, assaying 6 pennyweights, con-
centrated 15ozs. to the ton.’’ This, The Bulletin
has no hesitation in saying, is, on the face of it,
an absurd over-statement of Luhrig’s success; his
process, on the figures given, would almost pro-
duce so much gold as to affect the price of that
metal. It has always been reckoned that by ordi-
nary stamper and after-treatment much over a
third of the gold contained (as per assay) in ordi-
nary quartz is lost; but if we have in Luhrig's
process a treatment which will disclose and save
(and at so ridiculous a price) ten times the amount
of gold shown by assay, we are on the verge of an
era when most of us will be able to boil our tea in
golden billies.
One night in the Victorian Assembly
would reduce the Speaker of the House of Com-
mons to a state of gibbering lunacy. Aghast at
the vulgar outrages upon hoary tradition, and the
ruthless trampling down of usages which the
British Constitution holds sacred, that august
personage would rush outside to see the heavens
fall. And when the heavens didn’t fall, and no
earthquake showed signs of coming off, the guar-
dian of British parliamentary etiquette would
totter up to the nearest policeman and ask to be
taken off to a padded cell before he became
violent. This is apropos to the awful fact that
Tom Bent recently referred, during a debate,
to the ex-Premier in the following plain, unvar-
nished terms: “As Gillies says, that is too thin.”
The history of the world, probably, contains no
other such instance of senatorial blasphemy being
fired off in this incidental manner. \Vhat is fame,
what is honour, what is a distinguished repre-
sentative position, when a short, fat person, who
quite recently figured as a Chief Minister of the
Crown, has to sit within his own stronghold and
hear himself casually referred to as “Gillies?”
One can now understand Duncan straining to
tighten the silken bonds which bind us to a dear
old mother country, where a man of his standing
can only be mentioned in the ’Ouse as “ the Right
Honourable the Member for Somewhere,” and
where an outbreak of irreverence like Bent’s would
bring the Sergeant-at-Arms upon the scene, and
send a thrill of horror through a sidy, old com-
munity. Meanwhile, it certainly looks as though
Victoria were going to the dogs.


It is most convenient to have a lirst-class
room near tram stopping places, where ladies
can rest themselves, and if they choose, have a
good cup of tea, or other refreshments. We
understand the Country Milk Go’s. “Cascade”
80 & 82 Elizabeth-street, near King-street, is just
the place; it is certainly the largest and best
conducted room in Sydney, and is getting quite
the fashionable resort for afternoon tea. We also
understand that ladies who so desire can have
parcels sent there so that they can call for them
just before taking the tram for home.*

Parkes: His Diary.
If we gml at Parkes once more it is not that
we think him any longer potent for evil, for he
is now so low (inasmuch as he has been spat
upon l>y his erstwhile press toadies) that he
must be regarded as impotent even for harm ;
but we refer to him again so that the historian
of the future should not be tricked by the
malicious ingenuity of his former friend, the
S.M. Herald, to found upon a recent column
of that ponderous sheet, any portion of the
Parkesian mythos which is certain to be
originated in a century or so from now. The
enlightened intelligence of the 20th or 21st
century will never grasp the fact that a people
to close to it in point of time could ever have
accepted Parkes as a symbol of what is great
in statesmanship or venerable in life ; and upon
the mere bald circumstance that he filled so
pretentious a place in the life of this day, these
centuries will base, almost of a surety, a belief
in his essential greatness. Upon the 8.M.
Herald, and other daily papers, the judgment
of the future as to Parkes will depend in a
large measure, but the historian of the coining
time, not informed as we contemporaries are,
of the revulsion of feeling which that journal
has experienced with respect to its whilom idol,
might take its late publication of what pro-
fess to be quotations, verbatim et literatim,
from Sir Henry Parkes’ Diary, as a proof that
it continues to view him with admiration and
respect. But we men of to-day, knowing how
Parkes has before now been the victim of the
Herald’s malignity when he has been excluded
from power, can interpret its recent action.
Unlike the annalist of the future, we are not to
be misled. When the leading journal of New
South Wales published, on the 28th October,
“ Extracts from Sir Henry Parkes’ Diary,” it
was not intending to honour him or to inform
the public mind. No, it was spitting upon him
with the coarse virulence which has ever
characterised it in its relations to creatures who
are down, in the hope that it would induce its
readers to add to the stream of expectoration.
We must confess that, with a success quite
exceptional for the Herald, the hope is likely
to be realised,
# *• #
There are some persons, possibly, who really
believe that the “ Extracts ” were genuine, but
they must be few 7 indeed, and not to be found
outside of Tarban Creek or the Opposition-room
in New South Wales Assembly ; for what man
possessed of his wits could, for a second,
believe that a statesman—we will apply the
term simply as a concession to the argument—-
would, after a defeat in the Legislature which
signified the annihilation of his “life-work”
and the destruction of everything that he held
worthy of honour, go home and extract from
the trough in his piggery such hogwash as this :
Monday, October 19. —“ Left home this morning at 8.S0,
and was at the office by 9 o’clock. Many things required
my attention. An Executive Council at 10, to wind up
Ministerial work. Ministers met in Cabinet at 11.30. I
explained that the motion on Thursday night was one
which, though it did not necessitate our retirement, fully
justified our considering and deciding upon the situation
for ourselves ; we had gone as far as men could go without
losing all sense of honour and self-respect to meet the
unreasonable demands of the so-called Lahour-party, and
that 1 could not see that we were called upon to go fur-
ther. As we had not a majority without their inconsis-
tent votes, coupled with their rapacious demands, the
course best calculated to’maintain our own reputations,
and to serve the public interest and the true Cause of Par-
liamentary government, was to tender our resignations.
My colleagues unanimously concurred in this view. . .
The Assembly met at the usual hour. There was a large
attendance of members, and the spaces for strangers were
crowded. In a few quiet words I announced tur resigna-
tion, and went home happy.”
Anri the next day we have this gorgeously
bogus rhapsody : —
THE DAY AFTER.
Tuesday, 20th October.—Rose this morning a little
before 5 o’clock, after a long night’s refreshing sleep. As
soon as I went outside, my dog, “Maori,” came to greet
me, and the squirrels were playing among the boughs in
their ample cage. 1 went on to the front verandah and
sat down, with the waters of the bay all bright before
me. The sun was rising on the smoke-hazy city like a
disc of dazzling fire ; the peacock in his gorgeous robes
was sitting on the old lawn gate ; and three yards from
me the blackbird was singing his sweet matin songs. How
different from the weary waking after a tumultuous night
in the Assembly ! And, with God’s blessing, I may have
many such mornings. ... At home before six. What
a blessed change !
Absurd: not even the intelligence of the
average Herald editor could swallow so
monstrous a pill. The Herald may be im-
posed upon (as, we believe, it was a week or
two ago by alleged quotations from a diary of
1788) ; but it surely could not accept the
foregoing as authentic pages from an ex-
Premier’s diary.
# # *
For, it must be apparent even to the immature
perceptions of the Herald, that a statesman
of Napoleonic ambitions, retiring from his
Waterloo, would not dally with dogs, nor
would he have an eye to the playful squirrel
while his mind was occupied in tracing an
analogy between his position and that of the
coon who had just come down to Davy Croc-
kett’s gun. So distinguished a man would not
have been contemplating the rising sun; his
thoughts would have been fixed upon the setting
orb ; he would have seen in the peacock’s plum-
age evidence of the truth of the old superstition
that for the man who owns one of those
lustrous birds there is shame in reserve, and
he would have had no ear for the matin melody
of the blackbird, but would have been, perforce,
compelled to listen to the cynical gibe of the
kookaburra. For him there would have been
no enjoyment in the sense of freedom after re-
straints to which he had reluctantly yielded,
but only that biting consciousness of defeat,
and the poignant recognition that, after all, he
must be worthless and accursed by gods and
men, if this !—this ! ! this ! ! 1 was the end of
a lifetime of effort. 4
# # x? *
It is the paltriest, most imbecile of frauds,
this attempt to delude, not the judgment
of the present, but the judgment of the future.
There is nothing so contemptible, nothing
so immoral, as manufactured evidence ; it is
a species of forgery designed to deceive and
defraud the unborn. For the creatures who,
conscious and of malice aforethought, resolve
to mislead posterity, no term of reprehension
can be too strong, and their performance is
all more contemptible because it is so
wretWtedly done. Had the Sydney Morning
Herald been anxious to do the fair thing, and
the square thing, and really desirous of showing
to the present generation, and to posterity,
what sort of meditations a great statesman
indulges in in the sanctified hearing of
The dear confessor of his diary’s page,
it would not have given us so superficial a
composition as these supposititious extracts. It
would have shown us—and the future —the
great man in his agony. It would have quoted
from the “ extracts ” of that October morning
expressions which, like the salt tears of remorse,
burn the surface they fall upon ; and, after that
natural venting of the overburdened spirit, it
would have given us a cool, calm, clear analysis
of the causes which had led to the catastrophe.
It would have given us reflections which were
the mintage of a great heart in the crisis of its
history ; it would have inspired in the mind of
the most sceptical that purifying quality of
thought which must arise on witnessing the
prostration of the noble and the great. Every
reader of the Herald would then have ex-
claimed, “Fallen, but how great!” and not
“ Fallen, but how unutterably despicable !”
* * *
No, when the S. M. Herald wishes to pub-
lish to the world Parkes’ genuine diary let it
print —if it dare—the entry he made when
Wentworth lashed him from the hustings in
1848 ; of that morning in ’56 when all Sydney
rang with Homeric laughter at the instalment
of his autobiography which he published in that
day’s Empire ; of that evening when the hunger-
stricken wife of his employee, who had worked
for him without wage, was turned away from
his door penniless ; of the afternoon of that day
when Sir Alfred Stephen addressed to him
the one phrase which, of the thousands that
have fallen from the Judge’s lips, will survive
his death. Let the journal do this, and let it
quote—if it dare— Parkes’ own account of the
evolution of the Kiama Ghost, and of the in-
famous Treason Felony Act; let it publish his
reflections the night when Edward Butler
died, robbed, not of money this time, but of
that position of dignity and honor which was
the due reward of his labours ; and let it give to
the world that transcript of the broken-hearted
governess’s letter which was addressed to him on
the collapse of the Australian Mutual Prudential
Assurance Society. When it does this and
these, then we can believe that the Herald
is deriving from the only authentic source,
Parkes’ Diary. Till it does, we must accuse
it of being the instrument of an imposition, or
—we will not deny it the saving qualification—
the ridiculous tool of a shallow impostor. We
can only acquit the S.M.H. of originating a
fraud at the expense of arraigning its intelli-
gence. Perhaps, after all, the possibilities of
the one case are as likely as the other. The
great law of continuity in life demands that as
in life so in death shall the man be. Parkes in
political death could not help but be as he was
in political existence, a superlative fraud.
Both Houses of fS.A. Parliament have
passed a Cremation Bill. On the bill being
assented to by the Governor, Government will be
urged to build an Adelaide crematorium.
At Bathurst (N.S.W.) Railway-station,
newsboys are charged twopence each before being
permitted on the platform to try and sell their
papers. Scores of curiosity-gazers, who don’t sell
newspapers to the travelling public, are admitted
to the platform free of charge.
Great Britain and France have about an
equal population, in round numbers, 39 millions.
Yet France boasts of 10 millions of voters under
manhood suffrage, while Great Britain has only
0 millions, British registration laws disfran-
chise quite 2 millions among the working-class
voters alone.
Austin Bid well, the Bank of England
forger, has now been in eraol 18 years, and has yet
to serve another 18 months of his “imprisonment
for life.” Thus, though sentenced for a mere
offence against property, he has seen go out into
the world free five murderers, sentenced to death
since the date of his conviction.
Of course the N.S.W. Executive is going
to hang (“as a deterrent ”) old Dalton, the grey-
haired wretch who murdered his wife with a
branding-iron in a fit of anger, or madness, or
drunkenness, or all three mixed. The bodies of the
men hanged in England for such offences during
the last few years would, if laid head to foot,
stretch round the Melbourne Cup course, and yet
hangman Berry’s knuckly fingers are always full
of human neck.
Four hundred and fifty Sydney tailors,
mostly members of the Journeymen Tailors’
Union, struck work on Monday. A number of
non-society tailors threw in their lot with the
strikers, and the union was augmented by 50 new
members on the first day. The immediate cause
of the strike has been the refusal of many of the
employers in what are known as “first-class
shops” to agree to anew “log ” —a revised scale
of trade charges—submitted by the unionists, who
had been suffering under many grievous evils,
chiefly due to “sweaters.” The case having
been laid before the Trades and Labour Coun-
cil, that body determined to call upon all
its affiliated unions to assist the tailors, should
a strike be forced upon them. Some of the
employers gave press representatives perfectly
gorgeous statistics of wages-rates paid to their
employees week after week, a responsible member
of one large firm stating that coat-hands earned
“ from <£2 10s. up to as much as .£5 ss. and even
more ” per week. The men explain that an occa-
sional wage-entry of £5 for a week’s payment
was quite possible, but that it meant a subsequent
“dead horse,” as in the following week the
employee would be completing work that
had actually been already paid for. Further,
such occasional big payments usually include tbe
wages of assistants, apprentices, or other home-
helps, by which amounts they have to be dis-
counted.' They state their average wages at <£llßs.
per week, and “in the busiest portion of the
year, when the men were working 10 hours a
day, £2 Bs. 4d. per week.” The increases asked
for amount chiefly to advances of only two and
three per cent; in one case, to 10 per cent. ; a,nd
in another, to 13 per cent. The Tailoresses’ Union
has decided to support the men on strike, and will
further draft a “log” for women in the trade.
Eleven “second-class” and two “first-class”
firms have agreed to the men’s new log, and work
has been resumed in those establishments. Other
“first-class’’establishments are showing signs of
giving way to the demands of the men, which
certainly appear reasonable. If the tailors and
tailoresses handle their forces judiciously, they
should be able to exterminate the pernicious
sweater in Sydney.

Viewed bye and large, “Robbery L ndor
Arms ” resembles chaos more than any other
operation of nature. It certainly has no resem-
blance to Bold re wood’s book, but has a strong
likeness to “ The Sundowner,” “ The Sunny
South,” and other plays by George Darrell,
indeed, since beholding Alfred Dampier play
“Starlight”—“Limelight” would be a more
appropriate designation —many folks imagine
that he must" be George’s long-lost brother.
Possibly, however, they purchase their wigs,
boots and breeches, grease-paint, &0., at the same
emporium. This play is pervaded by a number or
individuals selected apparently more in conse-
quence of their capacity to fill out tight-fitting leg-
coverings than their histrionic capabilities, and
several somewhat inaudible females. Neverthe-
less, the acting, all round, is quite up to the
necessities of the drama. Some of Tischbauer s
scenery, particularly the “Rocky Rises” and the
“ Hill over-looking’Racecourse,” is very good. A
big house gathered to greet the long-lost Dampier
on first night, and he was tumultuously received.
The season lasts for some weeks, and the gap
between it and the pantomime will probably be
filled in with the MacMahon’s _ Opera Co., who
open in Composer Rice’s “Corsair,” By the way,
the Dampier girls’ chief improvement has been in
the way of looks since they last saw Sydney,
though Lily sits a horse fairly well. Alfred
Dampier himself doesn’t put his fine voice to such
good use as of yore, and gets about rather slowly
for a hunted man. On first night the coach
sticking-up scene was poorly managed, and didn t
“ sensate ” worth a cent.
* as * *
“Sithors to Grind” hasn’t had an edge
put on it in Sydney since Leitch’s Criterion
season of 1888. According to bill it is by “George
Ralph Walker,” but the two latter names in reality
spell Leitch. In this revival, the actor-author,
of course, plays his old part of Stammers, the
umbrella-mender and scissors-grinder, a character
which bubbles over with pathos, permeated by the
genial humour of the older school of play-writing.
Nellie Lyons is very well-suited as the heroine,
Eleanor Stammers; Frank Cates plays Lord Harry
Temple, and Dick Stewart is Ted Bluff, the
blacksmith. Other characters are ably handled,
and Mr. W. J. Wilson’s scenery is up to his usual
high standard. It is very unfortunate, not only
for George Leitch, but also the Sidney public, that
the influenza has precluded him from staging
“ The Light that Failed,” as Jennie Lee is due at
the Garrick on Saturday night with “ Run Wild,”
and the event will have to be postponed indefi-
nitely.
m* * *
According to Frank Emery, the Gaiety
Co.’s Sydney send-off was the most enthusiastic in
his recollection. The first outburst came when
Ethel Blenheim fooled the gallery into thinking
her the original Nellie Farren. When they got
Ethel full-face the cheers changed into yells for
Nellie, who, unhappily, came not. Leslie’s
entrance was the signal for cheers, interpolated
with a chorus of “ He’s a jolly good fellow, ” which
lasted for some minutes and culminated in
demands for “ Killaloe.” Storey was also in
great demand. Danby was let off with
“ Bally hooley,” sung to a gallery chorus, and “So
English, you know.” Sylvia Grey and Florence
Levey were also overwhelmed with flowers, and
the show concluded after eleven with the singing
of “ Auld Lang Syne,” followed by a brief speech
from Leslie, in which he regretted the absence of
that “ kindly spirit ” now “ crying her eyes out at
home,” Nellie Farren.
** * *
“Society’s” most faithful feature is its
depiction of Bohemian life at the “Owls’ Roost.”
For instance, the noble journalist wants to borrow
five shillings for cab-hire. The man he “whispers”
says: “Haven’t got it, dear boy, but I’ll get it
for you.” The scribbler he sticks-up gives the
same reply, and the attempt to float a loan goes,
after this fashion, right round the table until it
gets within one of coming back to Lyndal—there
its peregrination is stayed by the pecuniosity of a
bearded boozer, awaiting change for a sovereign.
The assurance : “ That’ll be all right in a minute,
laddie,” is then sent round from mouth to ear, and
the needed coin makes the same circuit from hand
to hand five minutes later. The management
announce the re-appearance of George Titheradge,
now recovered from the influenza, on Saturday
next. Mr. Shine has already returned to cast a
ray of light on the proceedings.
** * *
Business has improved slightly with “The
Dancing Girl,” at Sydney Her Majesty s, audit
is just possible that, handled by people of genius,
the terpsichorist and the insufferably good, though
allegedly wicked, “ Dook,” would have proved
fairly acceptable to Sydney audiences. As it is,
the bull-dog, the breakwater, the allegories, the
godly father, and the impressionable, though
puritanical youth, are received nightly with loud
outbursts of silence from the house. “ Fedora”
goes up on Friday for Miss Villiers’ benefit.
* *
We learn from Bondi that some weird,
underground mystery, known as “The Caves,”
has been laid on, but whether Wyburd has
tunnelled through to the Mammoth cave of Ken-
tucky, or has simply discovered a subterrannean
passage to Jenolan, we are not yet in a position
to state.
** * *
“The Jonquille,” a drama written by the
young South Australian, Guy Boothby, was
placed on the boards of the Adelaide Royal for
three nights as a benefit season to the author, who
proceeds to England. The play is founded upon
incidents in connection with the French Revolu-
tion, and undoubtedly possesses merit enough to
give it a fair show in London, if young Boothby has
the luck to get one of the metropolitan managers
to put it up. Miss Maud Williamson played the
title-role on its recent production and gave a most
artistic rendering. The remaining characters
were in the hands of local amateurs. It is little
credit to Boothby’s friends that they failed to
accord him more support in the matter of attend-
ance. Gerald and Duff have opened in “Human
Nature.” Also another amateur performance of a
local work has been given in the Albert Hall by
the members of the Harmonic Society, the cause
of the trouble being a comic operetta called “The
Windmill,” by M. Henzenroder, an Adelaide
musician. It is a tuneful little thing, and the man
with the long name deserves congratulation.
*» * *
“Faust,” at Melbourne Alexandra, is the
best-paying thing yet staged by the Simonsens.
The big barn has been crowded since Friday last,
and as the double cast makes consecutive per-
formances easy it is certain that the opera could
run for another fortnight. It is difficult to say
which of thetwopriraas,Guidottior Cuttica, makes
the better Margherita, although the odds are well
in favour of Cuttica. Their interpretations are
both clever, though widely divergent, and the
audiences like one as well as t’other. Cuttica
Signor is far and away a better Faust than little
Vilalta, who can’t act the text well enough to keep
himself awake. The badly presenced Sisco was
the first Valentine, and scored bigger things in
the death-scene than did Melossi, the more
pleasing-looking soldier of the second night,
lorio andTraviaglini were the Mephistopheles, and
here the last-named proved himself the superior.
Signorinas Mattioli and Castiglioni were respec-
tively the Siebel and Marta of every evening,
and do them well. The picturesque ending of
the opera has, unfortunately, been cut out. Old
Nick and Faust throw Margherita on to a heap
of straw and walk off, and an Uncle Tom’s Cabin-
like apotheosis ends the show. The satire of
Mephistofele singing his music in a cathedral to
the accompaniment of a church-organ does not
strike the audiences much. “ Faust,” with a few
one-night shows thrown in, will probably hold on
to the end of the season, which now draweth ex-
tremely nigh. Guidotti benefits to-night(Thursday)
with the last performance of “ Ernani.”
** * *
Last Friday, the Cockney Burlesque Co.
lent their faces to J. C. Williamson, and a
very long bill, beginning with “Kerry, ’’ ended
with a speech by James Cassius himself.
Some costermonger songs were sung by
Leslie, and the other Gaieties filled in. The
house was very full on Saturday, th® first
night of the revival of the “Old Guard.” The
ballet is as good as ever, and an evidently new
girl in dove-coloured pants knocks the bald-heads
every time. Elton is still the circus Mayor, and
the well beloved Howard Vernon shines as the
Marquise. Ida Osborne and Florence Young
hold up their old loads. The show is well known
everywhere just now, and there is nothing new to
say of it except that Gilbert and Sullivan would
be much more welcome than this hotch-potch of
fooling and legs and skirt-dances. G. and S. are
the only builders of comic opera extant who never
depended on the legs of strange girls for the sup-
port of their work.

** * *
“ A Night Off” has replaced “New Men
and Old Acres” at the Bijou. Mr Brough, as
Professor Babbitt, is sensibly funny, and Anson,
Sass, Ward, Corlesse fill up the men parts of a
remarkably strong cast. Mrs. Brough and Miss
Temple, as two of the nice gals of the professor’s
househould, took well, and gave pleasure to all,
and were also encored, whatever that may mean.
Annie Taylor, as the other member of the house-
hold, is funny enough for a religious paper. Miss
Bronton makes a good Mrs. Babbitt, and the
scenery is very fine.
#* * *
Other shows by the dozen have been run
together for Cup week. The Gaiety and Victoria
do good business, likewise the Exhibition Concert
and a variety show at Olympia, the scene
of the recent beauty-show, is propounding nigger
conundrums to fair houses. Wirth’s Circus is
still located at Swanston-street, and the “Cor-
sair,” at the Opera House, is keeping on its feet
without much trouble. The Faust Family are at
the Waxworks, and Phil. Stuart is extending the
accommodation of his cash-box.
«* * *
Snazelle opened at the Athenaeum on
Saturday for a season extending over race-week.
“Enoch Arden” is finely reproduced, and the
boss one-man-host-in-himself enacts “Faust” in
71 minutes. Miss Snazelle and Messrs. Langdale
and Hawkins fill up a good bill.
* * * *-
The new Cyclorama of the Siege of Paris
was opened last Friday afternoon. The building
is much larger than Waterloo, and being situated
in Bourke-street, just above the Bijou, is likely to
injure the receipts of the theatres. The Siege of
Paris is a much finer landscape painting than
Waterloo, but the figures are not so excellent all
round. Manager Forster lectures at the new show
as well as at the old one.
At Poverty Point.
The marmalade modestly ladled out by Fred. Leslie to
the absent Nellie Farren, on the occasion of the Gaiety
Co.’s last appearance in Sydney, was natural enough.
It is stated, on most unreliable authority, that Leslie’s
affection for Nellie is purely filial—that in point of fact
she is his mother. It is also averred that Frederick is
Nellie’s youngest son. '
Williamson’s Ilip Van Winkle is, in the opinion of many
people, a livelier and more effective performance than
Jefferson’s. In truth, Jeff, became very tedious in his
famous character after playing it a few thousand
thousand times, and elaborated his conception until it
was uncommonly like an artistic nuisance.

Woodfield’s hands are much against him in tenor lover
parts now-a-days. Haymaking, potato hoeing, and other
inisesthetic toil upon the “farm,” has turned their early
whiteness into a rich, brown chocolate colour.
Nilie Stewart’s London engagement resembles a spot
of ironmould on a dude’s shirt-front. It won't come oft'.
What The Bulletin said about Christie Murray’s acting
is echoed in the London Referee, which recently
remarked: “Mr. Murray in his own person shows how
unfair it is to rank acting with those professions for
which men have to qualify themselves by long and
severe study; for lie has mastered the art in no time,
and after his brief experience in Australia he has come to
England an uncommonly good actor, within limitations,
of a character part.”
William Rignold as the showman to Handsome Jarge’s
hero in “ Lights o’ London ” reminds one that the same
two characters were first played by another pair of
brothers—Wilson and George Barrett.
LINES ON A CORYPHEE.
She was such a bright little minx,
Though also the greatest of flirts—
Her legs were as long as her drinks,
And her temper as short as her skirts.
Majeroni was a good sort and open to conviction. On
one occasion he sent for a Bulletin theatrical critic with
the idea of calling him bad names, and after five minutes
of earnest conversation in the managerial office he planked
his hand into the august paw of the journalist and apolo-
gised for ever having doubted the honesty and personal
excellence of such a nice young man.
When the anti-Wagner hysterics were rampant recently
in Paris over the production of the opera “Lohengrin,”
M. Lamoureux, the chef d'orchestre, before commencing
one particular rehearsal, placed a revolver conspicuously
on his desk, and said to the assembled musicians“l
have received from someone among you a letter menacing
me with death. lam not going to try to find out who is
the author, but the first man who moves out of his
place .” The rehearsal passed off with silent smooth-
ness.

“I have read with much astonishment,” writes a Lon-
don correspondent, “ the gushing accounts in the Sydney
papers of Miss Frances Saville’s reception in England. For
ray part I have never heard her name mentioned, and
though I have enquired of a great many Australians no
one of my acquaintance knows anything about her Lord
Canngton and Lady de Grey may have given a large
matinee at Covent Garden in her honour, and Gus Harris
may have been so enchanted with her voice that he at
once offered her an engagement on fabulous terms • but
these things I can assure you are now read of for the first
tune by many of those whose business it is to know all
that Australians of any note are doing in England.”
