ANOTHER GOLD MEDAL AWARDED THE J. C.
AYER CO.
The J. C. Ayer Company, who were awarded
the gold medal for their medicinal preparations
exhibited at the Barcelona International Exhi-
bition, have just received notice that their medi-
cines also gained the gold medal at the Exhibition
recently closed at Kingston, Jamaica.
These awards are evidences of the high repute
in which the preparations of the J. 0. Ayer
Company are held in foreign countries.— Courier,
Cowell, Mass.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Melbourne Cup was only half a surprise
this year, though few of the venerated turf-tipsters
(with the exception of “Nemo,” who bull's-eyed the
winner) placed the son of Malua anywhere nearer the
winning-post than to label him “ dangerous.” However,
the numbers went up thus:—
Malvolio .. „ 1
Sir William .. ~ 2
Strathmore .. 3
Malvolio went backwards in the betting at latest dates,
though his first turf performance was the beating of
Highborn, who afterwards ran second to Carbine in last
year’s Cup. Malvolio, later still, won the Haw'ksburn
Handicap in brilliant fashion, and his preparation for
his present victory was in the capable hands of the
Caulfield trainer, J. Redfearn. His sire, Malua, won
the Melbourne Cup as a 5-year-old with fist. 91b. ; Malvolio
is 4 years, and carried Bst. 41b. His dam was Madcap.
S.r William, 5 years, by Sir Modred—Vesper, carried
Bst. 71b., and a heap of Sydney money. The stable-E.
Keys’s—had consistently backed him all along, and
“ Asmodeus,” of The Leader, considered him the best
handicapped horse of the whole of the 163 original candi-
dates, and tipped him amongst a quartet of winners.
Strathmore, the Derby winner, by Nordenfeldt-Ouida,
was the likeliest among the 3-year-olds, and a lot of Cup
money was put up on him late in the day.
# # #
The Argus prints a letter, complaining that the
V.R.C. distributes complimentary tickets ad libitum,
and that all the front seats in Flemington Grand Stand
are filled with dead-heads to the detriment of the paying
public. The accusation is obviously absurd. Dead-
heads at Flemington are mainly pressmen, a class of in-
dividuals who certainly don’t go dow n to the course for
the purpose of vegetating on benches from 12 to 5 o’clock.
Ladies, moreover, enjoy almost a monopoly of the
Stand on Cup and other big days, and although it may
be that the bearers of ladies’ tickets or ladies passed in by
members (each of whom can escort two bonnets through
the turnstiles) do take up a considerable portion of Stand
accommodation, these are not dead heads in the strict
acceptation of the term. They simply represent the
womankind interest in membership of that Club, and are
there by right. As for the insinuation that complimentary
tickets are too cheap at the V.R.C; office, no one
acquainted with the smart business policy of Byron
Moore and the tough personality of Miles, who is the
actual distributor of press passes, will give ear to such a
howl. Surely, too, the phenomenal success of the V.R.C.
is a guarantee that the right of entrie isn’t chucked about
promiscuously, just as the prosperity of any other sort of
show is conclusive evidence of the free-list department
being wisely administered. No place of entertainment in
Australia gets so much newspaper puffing at so compara-
tively little cost, in paid advertisement and press tickets,
as Flemington, and no racecourse in the world is
patronised by such an immense proportion of people who
never read sporting papers. The V.R.C. management
have grasped the fact that a brief notice in a trade jour-
nal which meets the eyes of many non-horsey people
is better worth acknowledging with a free ticket than
are ten columns of stuff in a racing paper whose
readers are already interested and nosted-up in turf
matters. It is clear enough to the initiated that this
Arous letter was inspired by, or anyway written in the
interests of, a rival racing-club, the management whereof
has acquired a reputation for being stupid as regards the
distribution of passes, and would like to induce the
V.R.C. to chop away a number of its press supports.
* * #
The rain came down like a wolf on the fold and
took all the starch out of Derby Day at Flemington. A
few girls in muslins degenerated into bundles of wet rags
long before closing time, whilst one pallid young female,
probably a bride, who insisted upon prowhng round the
paddock in a complete snow-tinted costume, finished
off by white kid boots, and stockings of the same hue,
looked like a mud-stained ghost. The weather was
beastly.
# # «
Unfortunately, the tempest did its worst during
the half-hour when horses were being saddled for the
Melbourne Stakes. Very few' people ventured into the
paddock to take stock of the starters and see how the
market champions for the Cup were popping up. It was
sufficient to stand afterwards under an umbrella and
cheer Marvel on his damp and dismal way. As neither
of the first two horses was to be a runner for the Cup, the
excitement following the Stakes was mild as compared
with previous years. Several of the competitors seemed
to be knocked out of the coming handicap by their poor
performances, and two or three of the others stood as
they were on the assumption that they had merely
been brought out for a wetting. Then Pigeontoe
got home easily through the dirt for the Hotham
Handicap, and was promptly pounced upon as a
certainty for the Cup. By this time, Pigeontoe may be
the hero of that event—on the other hand, he may not.
In any case, the big horse doesn’t look like a first-class
animal, but like a useful horse trained to the minute, and
calculated to show' to advantage over soft ground. Judg-
ing from appearances before the race, the Derby was a
match between Stromboli and Strathmore, the former
showing more breed and style than the latter, but behav.
mg like an ill-tempered cur in the paddock. The result
proved that Strathmore’s win at Caulfield was accom.
phshed on his merits, for the Nordenfeldt colt got
home without being once threatened with the whip.
Then a filly with a heathen-sounding name won the
Maribyrnong Plate. She didn’t look like a youngster
who will train into a real good one, nor were any of
the others especially striking to the eye. Probably they
are all moderate, like last year’s starters for the same
race. By the time the Coburg Stakes came off, most of
the company—and the attendance must have been far
below the average—were hurrying home to dry them-
selves. Yet punters wore pleasant faces, despite the
damp. Four warm favourites had rollicked home in
succession.
» * •
Mr. “Reggie ” Allen evidently believes in trying
to make his horses earn their oats. His three-year-old
Juan has been doing a deal of racing lately, and at
Warwick Farm on Saturdaj', after Juan had - won the
Farm Stakes, he was started in the next race with a 71b.
penalty, when he ran third.
* * #
Melbourne Sportsman published about six con-
flicting tips for the Cup in its two issues of last week, and
then had the impudence to inform confiding readers that
it would burst forth with another budget of “ final selec-
tions ”on the Monday before the race. For the reductio
ad absurdum of racing prophecy one must go to the
professional sporting paper, which sets up a succession of
dummy idols for bookmakers to get a bit out of, and
howls like a suffering dog if it can stumble across the
winner in the course of four-and-twenty tries.
* * *
The Melbourne Cup run for on Tuesday last
was the 31st—the race having been established in 1861.
In 1885, 35 horses started, the smallest number being in
1863 (Banker’s year), when only 7 faced the flag.
* * #
Melbourne Cup round again reminds you of the
Pantheon of the Departed. Take off your hats to the
manes of Bagot, Standish, Goldsbrough, Tait, White, Ike
Hind, Blackwood, Chirnside, Waldock, Morrison, with
the jocks. Robertson, Nicholson, M’Grade, and the host
butchered on the Steeplechase courses.
* *d *
Mr. George Hill had a deal of luck in picking
up that truly-named horse Marvel. Originally owned by
a syndicate, who might have made a fortune by running
the Marvel “ straight,” they, or some of them, overdid
the clever business, and the horse was disqualified when
Mr. Hill purchased him for a song. Mr. Hill’s influence
soon caused the removal of the disqualification.
* * #
Tummy’s biggest bid for an important handicap
up to date was made in the Cambridgeshire. His
three-year-old colt Derelict ran third.
* * *
Britishers will doubly regret the expatriation of
Musket now that it has been proved that, in addition to
his powers as a getter of racing phenomena, the flyers in
turn are sireing racehorses of the first water. On‘Satur-
day, Nordenfeldt (for whom Sam. Hordern has refused
£20,000) was responsible for two winners-Strathmore in
the Derby, and Steadfast in the Maiden Plate.
* * #
The much-boomed George Dawson’s science
stood no show with the rushing but clever Williams’
onslaughts in Melbourne on Derby night. Williams
started business so soon as the scratch was toed, ar.d
dodgfe and duck as he might Dawson was unable to
escape. Williams smashed him about until the fourth
round, when Dawson was knocked insensible. Dawson
is so well-conducted a lad that many will be sorry for his
bad luck ; but he has been ridiculously extolled as a
fighter, despite the fact that he has on more than one
occasion narrowly escaped defeat, notably at the hands of
Dummy Mace. Williams now goes to America and Eng-
land to assert his claim to the world’s light-weight cham-
pionship.

Dempsey, hero of of 43 successive victorious battles, by
his ’Frisco admirers, realised 8000 dols.

Young Rush, a son of the well-known “ Mick ”
Kush, who used to have a say in aquatic championships,
w £ n his “I 8 * 1 race of any moment at Grafton on Saturda\,
when he defeated Malone for £9O a-side. Rush is 19 years
of age and weighs 11 atone in condition. Bill Beach also
hasi a son, a powerful-looking lad, who is said to be the
makings of a good man, so that it is quite on the cards
that the old-time aquatic names will be upheld for another
generation at least.
* # #
A Sydneyite some time since imported some
game-fowl from England, and after repeated pesterings
from a man who demanded a clutch of eggs, he handed
them over, having first taken the precaution of giving
them what he considered a real good parboiling. Eater
on he met the importunate party and asked how things
were, and was informed that 11 eggs had hatched out of
the 18. He treated the information rather as a joke until
some 12 months afterwards, when the cadger “ beat him
bad ” at a show.
Derby Day.
Everybody, writes “ Sappho Smith,” from Mel-
bourne, must have echoed Lady Jersey’s chirrup, as she
entered the V.R. enclosure at Flemington, on Derby Day,
What a quantity not number—“of people I” for
though “ the day was cold and dark and dreary ” the big
racecourse was mobbed as usual, and late comers had to
stand up and gaze forlornly at the crowded stands.
But, despite the weather, there were several cynosures in
the rag-line abroad, though my flesh did sometimes creep
at the sight of beauty got up in cambric with a stiff
nor’-easter blowing, Bill.” Beginning at the top of the
social column, I noticed first the unpopular Lady
Hopetoun—pale and bored (did she hear, I wonder, the
unfavourable stage asides whispered about her all round?:
and clothed in a black lace and silk skirt with visite o!
black lace, with gold embroidered yoke and high lace
collar, bonnet in black lace and jet, with ostrich
aigrette and crown of crushed pink roses, suede
gloves, and a single white gardenia at her throat
INext to her sat Lady Willoughby, Gov. Hope-
toun s itinerant middle - aged cousin, who wore a
gown of heliotrope cotele trimmed with tinsel brocade
in gold, mauve and silver, the blouse front of cream lace ;
mantle in black woollen with trimming of gold braid and
coronet bonnet in black lace; ostrich tips and gold
gimp; Lady Jersey- who seemed to enjoy herself
very much—wore blotting-paper pink crGpe cloth
with basque and cascade front of cream lace
and trimmings of floral applique in greenish grey
and silver, flat bonnet composed of pearls and gold
spangles and trimmed at either pole with red currants
electric-blue Mother Hubbard wrap over all; Lady Kintore
encored the get-up she wore at Randwick Spring Meeting
a grey crepe cloth with grey pansies worked hero
and there, and trimmed with hands of pansies in
natural colours, sash at the back of green and pansy
ribbon, smocked vest of pansy surah, bonnet in gold
straw trimmed with pansies and a little brown bird in
front, a cluster of pansies on top of her grey gamp, which
had an ivory cockatoo clawing up the handle. The other
lady in the V.R. box wore mauve with a darker dot
relieved with yellow silk, hride-like bonnet in white
acacia. There was much enthusiasm and congratulatory
hand-shaking among the gubernatorial party when Strath-
more annexed the Derby and was led up to the V.R.-hox
for Lady Hopetoun to lie his blue necktie on. But the
perverse “colleen” declined the honour, and Gov. H.—
who looked really fagged out with having to do the offi-
cially smiling-agreeahle for two —elbowed his way through
the mob and fastened the ribbon on Mr. Wilson’s pretty
brown horse.
* # #
As the rain had soaked the lawn, and thin-soled
promenaders were restricted to the strip of stone pave-
way in front of the g.s., relieved, here and there, with
puddles of water, a lot of women stopped cramped up in
their seats, and judging toilettes was like trying to pick
out the prettiest peach-blossom on a crowded bough. No
colour predominated, verifying the suspicion that no hue
leads this spring, and the only novelty was a saucer-shaped
hat, with a little round flat brim, trimmed with a roll of
silk and a Mercury wing, which, though unbecoming to
most faces, yet found many followers. Miss Manning
(Sydney) wore a shrimp-pink crepe cloth, with shaggy
knobs of pink, trimmed witli ruby velvet; black lace hat,
trimmed with pink roses and black wings. Miss Hogg, in
cornflower-blue silk diagonally barred with black, blouse
front of blue crepe and a sort of Fiji apron in black bugles,
hat covered with blue feather, and gamp exactly match- •
ing, looked altogether good form ; Miss Roberts (Sydney ',
in lavender-grey lit up with yellow silk, had a Tudor cape
of fawn with green velvet yoke, black hat swarming with
yellow roses, yellow silk frilled gamp ; Miss Brush (Mel-
bourne) wore an artistic get-up of fawn lined over with
narrow, glittering lines, fulled sleeves, and front of sage
green crepe embroidered in tiny white flowers, trimmed
with bows of narrow sage-green velvet, hat of black
crinoline straw trimmed with green bows and black
feathers, parasol of the same material as the frock.
Miss Cox wore pale-grey with front of silk, white
hat trimmed with yellow ’santheraums ; Sylvia Grey wore
white and a mauve dot trimmed with panels and et
ceteras of mauve silk, hat covered with white feathers—
but one might as well attempt to diagram the sands on
the seashore as the myriads of women at Flemington ;
and I want to have a little gossip about the Derby man.’
There isn’t much variety about any Johnny, barring his
button-holer. This year cornflowers were the raging
vegetable, and all the jeunesse doree exemplified a blue
look-out in their coat-collars. One trio of blonde men
with blue eyes, blue ties, and blue nosegays a span across
were delightfully en suite. The men, especially the top-
hatted section, suffered more from the showers than the
women.
A Croydon (N. Q.) advice states that the
deep ground on that field is turning out well, but
that the labour-market there is over-supplied.
Sam Hordern has refused £20,000 for
Nordenfeldt. That sum would have been <£16,000
on his bargain of a few months ago—interest at
the rate of about 1200 per cent, per annum.

recorded in the English press:— ,- A successful
furniture-manufacturer, one Blundell Maple, gave
15,000 guineas for a three-year-old racehorse, and
6000 guineas for an unbroken colt.” ‘‘The baby
of a London fish-eurer’s assistant died of starva-
tion because it could get no nutriment out of its
famishing mother’s body.”

POLITICAL
POINTS
“ The hidden national will, concealed in
its abysses, yet ever alert.” — Whitman.
Now that Parkes has lost the political
situation it doesn’t interest him any more.
In England there is a Judge to every
800,000 people; in Victoria, one to every 2oo,ooo.
Barton : “I shall have sitting right
opposite to me in the House the most implacable
enemy of Federation, George Reid.”
O Shan assy : “ When Australian Federa-
tion comes on the board, Imperial Federation will
be drawn, as a herring, across the trail.”
Mr. Black, M.P., says that Dibbs will be
a more honest leader than Parkes. This is what
we call damning a man with faint praise.
Salomons has never sat in the N.S W*
Assembly. He opposed Sir Henry Parkes once
in East Sydney, and got a great drubbing.
D. Melville is the only member of the
Victorian Legislative Council who is openly in
favour of the One-man-one-vote principle.
It is a fair sample of Parkes’ innate
cussedness, that he resigned just in time to pre-
vent Dibbs and Barton seeing the Melbourne
Cnp.
Two members of the Dibbs Administration
are directors of the Sydney Star. G. R. Dibbs
wrote for the Sydney News in 1885 ; but now the
News writes down Dibbs.
J. P. Howe (Bourke) is perhaps the first
member of the N.S.W. Assembly who has been
rounded of a felonry. His seat will, no doubt, be
filled by a Labour representative.
The area of S. Australia is 243,244,800
acres.. 8,532,823 acres are held by freeholders.
Land not cultivated but enclosed 58,715,971 acres!
Land under cultivation, 2,649,098 acres.
During the reign of Queen Victoria the
hours of labour have been reduced by about 8 per
cent. That seems a lot until you consider that
the efficiency of labour has been increased about
25 per cent.
It is this time almost safe to predict that
Parkes is dead as a political leader. Everything
points to a N.S.W. coalition sooner or later, and
one of the chief objects of any coalition would be
to get rid of Parkes.
In one of the Australian Parliaments the
question is : When will lie resign ? He constantly
interrupts proceedings with hiccupy speeches and
the other day wrecked a valuable Bill by voting
against it while drunk.
At the general election the Sydney papers
entered into a conspiracy not to report the
addresses of Labour-candidates. The speeches of
Labour members are now reported very carefully,
and with crood judgment.
The Playford (S.A.) Government have
cirried a measure providing for State land-pur-
chase on a large scale. The measure is welcomed
by the landocracy, who are falling over each other
in the attempt to sell out.
St. James’ Gazette says Parkes’ dismissal
from office removes “ the only guarantee of safety
to British investors.” “Safety to British in-
vestors ” stands, in Imperial parlance, for
“ Federation under the Crown.”
The Tasmanian Parliament has just been
running a big syndicate session, everybody ac-
commodating everybody right and left. What a
blessing that all its members are known to be
impervious to palm-oil—and tips !
London Times expresses the opinion that
Sir Henry Parkes was “a dangerous financier,”
The Times should just read the list of moneys
borrowed by the old man from politicians whom
he subsequently appointed to the N.S.W. Legisla-
tive Council.
In South Australia, Tasmania, and N. S.
Wales the Commonwealth Bill is shelved for the
current session ; in Queensland it will probably
be shelved ; whilst in Victoria the Council has
disagreed with the Assembly’s amendment in
favour of One-man-one-vote being embodied in
the constitution.
C. C. Kingston, M. P,, one of the drafts-
men of the Commonwealth Bill, has changed his
opinions as to Australian Federation, and now
considers the present time inopportune for Aus-
tralian union. There is strong reason for believing
that nearly all the S.A. delegates now hold simi-
lar opinions.
The Tasmanian Assembly, although it has
recently enacted payment of members and trien-
nial Parliaments, lias thrown out a bill providing
for One-man-one-vote. In the Tasmanian Payment
of Members Act, strange to say, the first pay-day is
not fixed, and members are dnnning the Treasurer
for their maiden “pony.” The screw is £lOO a
year.
The Salvation Army has a good idea of the
power of sex. The lasses never attempt to trade
off War Crys with women, and the “soldier”
does all his canvassing with the nnregeneratecl
female, and never with the man. For this reason
it is a very rare thing to see a soldier in a pub.—
unless, of course, he gets his tipple at the jug-and-
bottle entrance.
No sane Government would take up
Parkes’ Electoral Reform bills except with the view
of appealing to the country on the question of
abolishing the Upper House. Dibbs should move
for the discharge of these hotch-potch measures
from the paper, and then bring down a short One-
man-one-vote Bill and defy the Council to touch
one word or one comma of it.
“In the morning,” once said Benjamin
Franklin, dealing with the property-qualification
question, “John Smith owned a donkey, and had
a vote. In the afternoon the donkey’s death
made John Smith voteless. It was, therefore, the
donkey which had the vote, and not John Smith:”
This is an ancient jibe, but it is still good enough
to heave at J. B. Patterson.
Griffith feels exceedingly savage at the pro-
spect of being driven out of office by a democratic
party under the leadership of Lilley. It is whis-
pered in the Queensland clubs that the Griffith-
MTlwraith party ventured to rebuke Sir Charles
Lilley for expounding democratic ideas at the
opening of the Brisbane Trades Hall, and since
then there has been trouble in the camp.
The Emperor of Germany ukased that, in
future, no man is to be so degraded as to live on
the earnings of any fallen woman. William is
apparently a good and honourable prig, but he
makes a mistake in thus tackling iniquity piece-
meal, as it were. It would be much more to the
point to issue a rescript to this effect : “It is
hereby ordered that original sin shall be abolished
within seven days.”

Sydney D.T. has published two leaders a
day on the Dibbs’ Ministry. The directors are, it
is understood, almost evenly divided on the fiscal
question ; and Protectionist Treasurer See is a
large shareholder in the paper. The Bulletin
ventures to think that if the D.T. were to devote
one leader a day to the political situation and the
other leader to the “encouragement of private
enterprise,” it would give complete satisfaction in
certain quarters in N.S.W.
How much leisure do the N.S.W. Labour
representatives spend in writing long letters to
the press? Nearly every Sydney paper publishes
a letter from a supersensitive democrat, who
cannot carry a few ounces of political mud with-
out acquainting the public with the fact. The
Bulletin rises to suggest that the Labour members
devote their leisure to the study of political tactics.
All this letter-writing is suicidal, and delivers
over the party into the hands of its enemies.

Samuel championing the Parkes-M'Millan deficit
in the Loudon press ;
Not a shilling of (N.S.W.) public money has been mis’
applied.
N.S. Wales may- be proud of its Agent-General.
A man who can coolly undertake the stupendous
task of defending all Parkes’ public expenditure
is far and away- better in that billet than a large,
fanciful, and ornate liar.
The British Colonial Office’s recommenda-
tion that the Admiralty should choose as far as
practicable unmarried officers only for service on
the Australian station throws a little light on the
artifices by which it is proposed to draw tighter
the “silken bonds” which link us to the Im-
perialism of a distant country. “Marry Austra-
lian women to naval officers,” says the Colonial
Office, “and the Australian families thus allied
will look to and consider England instead of their
native land.” Australia must haveher own navy-
The Auxiliary squadron is.a mere instrument of
political proselytism.
The New South Wales Government will
find itself in a tight place over the second volume
of “ The History of New South Wales from the
Records.” In the first place, the limit of time for
delivery of the second volume has long been ex-
ceeded, and then, again, the subscribers ordered
an original work, based on the records, by “G. B.
Barton,” they did not agree to accept a volume
which is made up of bald, dry official papers. And.
besides, the faculty of historical research cannot
be originated by the bidding of a Parkes.
As The Bulletin lately showed, the
N.S.W. “National” Association consists solely
of non-producers—of commission-agents, lawyers,
landrsharks, and fat capitalists—to the utter ex-
clusion of wage-earners. Recognising the absur-
dity of its position, the Association —why doesn’t
it call itself the “National Gang”? —now
seeks to symmetricise its story by lowering
the subscription from 2s. (id. to Is. and placing
some producers and hand-workers on its Council.
Too late, dear friends, too late. The cat is out of
the bag.
Parkes’ chance of getting a pension from
the present Parliament of N.S.W. is a very small
one.- Anticipating a defeat on the Commonwealth
Bill he courted a crisis, and handed over the
government to the Opposition. Since his defeat
he has never missed an opportunity of slating the
Labour members, and sowing the seeds of clis-
sensioi in the Assembly. lie conspires to bring
about a dissolution of the Parliament —that he
may do—and a dissolution of the Labour party —
which he will never do-with the sole object of
creating a plastic Parliament that will “do the
right thing by him.”
In Victoria, coffee, sago, cocoa, chocolate,
and salt are all admitted free of import duty, and
tea is taxed Id. per lb. ; inN.S.W., the imposts
run—coffee and cocoa, Gd. per lb. each ; sago, Id.
per lb. ; chocolate, 4d. per lb.; salt, 20s. per ton ;
tea, 3d. per lb. In N.S.W., carriages, jewellery,
silks, carpets, silverplate, and pianos all come in
free —because the poor man uses these things
never ; in Victoria, they are taxed—carriages,
£SO each ; jewellery and silks, 20 per cent, each ;
carpets, 25 per cent. ; silverplate, 2s. per oz.;
pianos, from £5 to £ls each. This has all been
pointed out several times before, but it will bear
rehearsing.
The contemptuous references in Parkes’
political diary—he keeps a personal diary as well
—to the “inconsistent votes” and “rapacious
demands” of the Democrats in the N.S.W.
Assembly, contrast very strangely with his views
of the people at the time he was nicknamed “the
arch-Anarchist ” by Wentworth : “ I would no
more truckle to the working classes than to the
highest; and at the same time I believe that
among the lowest class there is often to be found
the largest amount of virtue, the largest share of
those energies which are most valuable to a young
country, and on which every institution of the
country must depend.”
The Maoriland Parliament, according to
the veteran Alfred Saunders, M.H.R. who has
just completed his half-century of political life,
consists of—
ten lawyers, ten merchants, storekeepers or auctioneers,
ten large landowners or agents of land companies, run-
holders or land speculators, ten farmers, six journalists,
four Maoris, two brewers, two mine agents, one doctor,
one captain, one major, one pensioner, one shipping
agent, one contractor, one builder, one painter and
glazier, two working shoemakers, one working tailor, one
stonemason, one brass-fitter, one carpenter, one lamp-
lighter and four or five nondescripts. Thus three thou-
sand professional men are getting more representation in
the present House than 40,000 farmers. With all the talk
about Labour representatives there is not one farm-
labourer in the House, and only seven working mechanics
to represent 80 000 wage-earners. Ten lawyers have
ridden into Parliament on the backs of the working-men.
The Sydney papers have indulged in a
prolonged scream over the political union of Toby
Barton and G. R. Dibbs. Public virtue is the
burden of their cry, and yet these papers have
licked Parkes’ political boots for many years!
Toby Barton is politically unclean, not because ho
and Dibbs sat on opposite sides last month, but
because protection is imminent, and the inflow of
ads. is threatened. Toby Barton is sober now on
the question of the Commonwealth Bill ; it will
never see the light, but will be overshadowed by
important measures. Toby has cooled down on
the federation-under-the-Crowu business, but the
papers will not credit him witii a return to poli
tical sobriety.
The statue of Australia’s first chief gaoler
and hangman, Governor Arthur Phillip, for the
erection of which N.S.W. Parliament asiniuely
voted, under Parkesian pressure, £3OOO, will cost
at least £11,500, one of the last official acts of
-Parkes being the approval of additional expendi-
ture which will bring the cost to date to that
enormous sum. The Bulletin computes that,
since 1887, Parkes’ crude, not to say vicious,
dilletanteism has bled his country to the extent of
£123,000 — what with literary jobs, subsidies to
alleged art, and the attempts, cancerous (as are all
Parkes’ efforts in every direction and in every
department of public life) with his egotism, to
apotheosise such spawn of British officialdom as
has at one time or other polluted the Australias
by its presence.
Sir Henry Parkes has been silly enough
to publish some more leaves from his “political
diary.” Four or five years ago he was cajoled by
a smart reporter into doing the same foolisi
thing; and once more his vanity has been
ministered .to by a crafty pressman. For many
years Sir Henry has kept a diary of important
occurrences in his public life. Sir John Robert-
son was not aware that Sir Henry aped the
“eminent man” to that extent until a paper
printed an entry from the “ political diary.” Sir
John’s opinion of Sir Henry as a truthful chroni-
cler was not very high : “ Although some part of
the entry,” said the bluff Sir John, “agrees with
my memory of what took place between us, some
part of what is recorded never took place at all,
and the greater part of what did take place is not
mentioned in the diary. Thank God ! I can take
my own part without a diary, without any
duplicity, and without any double-dealing.”
Premier Munro’s four cabinet colleagues
in the Victorian Legislative Council are all
opposed to his One-man-one-vote policy, and
Munro is consequently confronted with an
insoluble portfolio problem. The Council, being
a sort of bastard elective body, claims full repre-
sentative privileges, and is practically indepen-
dent of the wrath of Governments, as the process
of changing the personnel of the chamber requires
six years for full completion, one-third of the
House retiring every two years. The obstinate
conservatism of the Victorian Legislative Council,
and its determined opposition to popular pro-
noucements, are causing democratic leaders to
ponder more find more over the respective merits
and demerits of bicameral v. unicameral constitu-
tions. Evils which the democracy cannot sweep
away must be tunnelled through and dynamited.
Thousands of lives are sacrificed annually
because mankind are not educated in the first
rudiments of hygiene and life-preservation. Simple
ailments are neglected. The debility increases,
and some fatal disease attacks the victim, and all
life’s pleasure departs, existence becomes a bmden,
. life a trouble, and all because mankind is so
apathetic and regardless of the signs nature sends
that the vital strength is declining. All cases
require immediate attention before it is too late,
and a course of Clements Tonic invariably has the
effect of immediately restoring perfect health.
Miss Eva Brown, daughter of Sergeant Brown,
Albury, N.S.W., writes on June 15, 1891“ The
following particulars of my case are furnished on
behalf of the afflicted, trusting they may be the
means of restoring others to health : For eighteen
months I was extremely debilitated, the doctors
being of opinion that I had outgrown my strength.
My disease is known in medical circles as anaemia
or poverty of blood. Pains invaded all parts of
ray body ; I suffered from general languor, extreme
weakness, listlessness, dejection of spirits, indigis-
tion, fickle appetite and disturbed sleep. My
'body presented a bloodless appearance; my lips
were pale and the skin like wax. I was under
several doctors who prescribed for me ; but their
treatment did not alleviate my sickness. I
imagined that my constitution was breaking up,
and as the disease progressed I became more,
nervous and hysterical. My sufferings were also
greatly increased by a shock to the nervous system
received last year, when a railway carriage in
which I was seated caught fire. When my case
was most critical and gave serious apprehensions
to my friends and myself, I started on a course of
Clements Tonic, which I took, in conjunction with
Dr. Fletcher’s pills. The first two bottles effected
a great improvement, and I could relish my food
again, and my general weakness and nervousness
vanished altogether. I took about twelve bottles
of Clements Tonic, and my pale face is now re-
placed with the hue of health, and my recovery is
as complete as it was speedy.” D. W. Carter,
Esq., of the same city, also writes ; “During the
past twelve months I have suffered from a general
breakdown of the system, and being of a bilious
disposition, I suffered acutely from indigestion
and its train of distressing effects, most particu-
larly sick headaches. Six bottles of Clements
Tonic and a few boxes of Fletcher's pills effected
a radical cure, and I shall always be glad to certify
accordingly.”

A real live Irish baronet is driving a cab
in Hobart —he says he can make more that way
than out of the title. Meanwhile his encumbered
estates are righting themselves, and his eldest son
will enjoy them and have a four-in-hand of his
own —unless the Deluge comes along.
Havelock Ellis, the distinguished author
of “The Criminal.” and the editor of Walter
Scott’s “Contemporary Science” series, lived at
Burwood, N.S.W., fourteen years ago, as a tutor.
He also spent some years in the Clarence, the
Bathurst, and the Liverpool Range districts.

Kaiser Wilhelm's announced crusade
against the fie-fies has no doubt a more or less
personal origin. His own physical infirmities are
a mark of his blood-connection with what Bis-
marck called “those scrofulous Guelphs.” And
his father, it is now well-known, did not die of
cancer—but of the disease in wliich Dr. Alfred
Fournier, of Paris, and Dr, Jonathan Hutchin-
son, of London, are specialists.
Stead's cause of quarrel with Dilke is not
merely that Dilke is alleged to have committed
adultery. Stead and God could forgive
adulterers. It is not merely that Dilke is alleged
to have told lies about his alleged adultery. Stead
—and God—could forgive even that. Dilke’s
crime is obviously that Dilke told lies to Stead.
There is no pardon under Heaven, either from
Stead or from God, for a crime of that sort.

A few months ago Mr. Allen, the repre-
sentative of a big publishing firm (Longmans),
was in Sydney, and Premier Parkes, profuse in
his attentions and hospitality to the man, asked
for a candid opinion of his poems—how many
copies would sell in London. The opinion was
candidly given. “I think,” said the expert,
“that you might expect five copies to be sold.
I mention five, because that was the number that
was sold of some poems of—er—about the same
sort.”
Our talented young friend, J. Le Gay
Erereton, of Sydney, has just published a
strong article in favour of vegetarianism in
New York Twentieth Century. As to the
argument of the orthodox, that they eat flesh
“ because God gave animals for food,” he remarks,
“ Fancy the Creator and moral governor of the
Universe a butcher, supplying Smith, Brown,
and Robinson with dead flesh !” The Pastoralists’
Association will have to suppress Erereton, who
threatens to ruin the market for fat stock.
Colonel Johnston, the deposer of Governor
Eligh, was buried in a vault on the Annandale
estate. He was detested by the “first fleeters.”
A story is told that some old hands determined
that his spirit should not rest. In those days it
was commonly believed that a spirit would never
rest once it was carried across the water. Not
long after Colonel Johnston’s death, according to
the story, his vault was opened, and the coffin was
carried down to the water-frontage of the estate,
and then rowed across to the shore of White Horse
Bay.

which A. P. Martin raised such a cook-a-doodlo-
doo some years ago, was to be found in a volume
of the poet’s verses issued at Mt. Garnbier, S.A.
ihe statement has been questioned, of course, by
someone in Mr. Martin’s interest. “The matter,”
remarks the correspondent, “ can easily be set at
rest by a reference to the family of Mr. Riddoch
(S.A.), on whose station Gordon w'as a stock-
rider.”
Parkes did once make a genuine bun-mot.
One night during his candidature for a seat in the
old N.S. W. Legislative Council, he was announced
to speak in the skittle-alley of a Sydney Sussex-
street pub., and a man dropped in about a quarter
of an hour after the advertised time of opening to
find only about nine or ten persons present, all
of whom had apparently been asked and had
refused to take the chair. On Parkes observing
the new-comer, he rushed forward, and, shaking
him enthusiastically by the hand, begged him to
preside. “But,” gasped the new-arrival, “I don’t
know you !” “ That’s all the better,” said ’Enry
—“ the others do /”
Hodder and Staughton, of London, have
issued a book under the title of “George Fife
Angas,” who, says his biographer, “ was one of
the fathers and founders of South Australia,
originated the South Australian company, the
Bank of South Australia, the National Provincial
Bank of England, and the Union Bank of Aus-
tralia, and whose foresight and shrewdness won
for Great Britain the possession of New Zealand
as a colony.” This all sounds very nice, but the
fact is that South Australia has been a rich father
to the now millionaire Angas family, who got
thousands upon thousands of acres of its most
beautiful land at half-a-sovereign an acre.
Dan. O’Connor, M.L.C., never tells his.
friends the story of Sara Bernhardt’s reception at
the Australia Hotel, Sydney. The reception was
rather a fiasco as far as the spokesmen for the
proprietary were concerned. Henry Gorman, a
director of the hotel company, and a good-looking
fellow, was the first to welcome the artiste; but
his remarks awakened no response. Then the
sombre-looking Dr. Garran addressed Sara; and
still no notice was taken of the speaker or the
welcome. But whilst Dan O’Connor was couching
a welcome in a hybrid tongue she caught sight of
a familiar face in the distance. Leaving the three
great men at the entrance, Sara advanced towards
her friend, and to Dan. O’Connor’s mortification
she put her hands on her friend’s shoulders and
said, “Ah! Mr. Moore, of the Toronto Hotel, I
know I’ll be so happy here.”
B. F. Underwood, the erstwhile distin-
guished materialist, of Boston, U.S.A., has
turned spiritualist. Same old story—the highest
type of inquiring intellect is like the Huma, the
fabled bird of passage which is always on tire
wing. The present tide of change is in favour of
Spiritualism and all that kind of thing, and per-
haps it is on that account that we find Stead pre-
paring a Christmas issue devoted solely to ghost-
stories. From our standpoint one thing is certain.
T he man to whom a “ghost ” appears and who is
quite sure that if he hits it on the head hard with
a half-brick its skull will be fractured, is sane and
nr no danger of losing his reason. On the other
hand, the man who, seeing an alleged ghost,
regards it as a visitor from the other world, may
go cranky any day. When you see a ghost, dear
friends, kick it in the shins.
Just to prevent Arthur Patchett Martin
in ins forthcoming “ Life of Lord Sherbrooke ”
(Robert Lowe) crediting Lowe with the author-
ship of some verses on Caroline Chisholm, the
philanthropist, which appeared in the Empire,
Sydney newspaper, under Lowe’s name, we may
say definitely that the lines in question, begin-

were written by the lately-deceased J. Sheridan
Moore. The first time the writer of this para-
graph saw these lines was as a cutting pasted
opposite Mrs. Chisholm’s portrait in the scrap-

district, Vic. “ You are looking at that extract,”
said the hostess: “I owe all I have to Caroline
Chisholm—l’m one of her girls. She-brought me
out and found me my husband.” The hubby was
worth at least a couple of hundred thousand then
Old Thomas Roadknight, septuagenarian
Victorian squatter, who has gone to select new
country in the next world, began Australian life
as a pound-keeper in Vandemonia. As “ Price
Waning ” remarked months ago, it is curious how
many of the first squatting families similarly
originated. An early-day pound-keeper had a
finer opportunity than any other man, bar a first-
rank official, of stocking unsettled country at
small—or no—cost. Roadkuight was one of the
party who went out in search of Gellibrand and
Hesse, who were lost in Western Victoria
while exploring. Another of the patty was
the Rev. Thomas Beagley Naylor, that great-
souled parson whom “ Charley ” Naylor,
of Bathurst, had the honor to call father.
On the route, about where the township of
Birregurra now stands, the search-party encoun-
tered a tribe of blacks. One of the men fired a
musket just as Naylor started a hymn—Naylor
always believed in pacific methods and lie thought
that music would soothe the savage breast. The
blacks bolted, and it remains in dispute to this
day whether Naylor’s singing or the musket re-
port made them skedaddle. Roadknight always
averred ’twas the hymn.
General Whichcote, “the very last Water-
loo officer,” as the London Times called him, who
died last August, had a narrow escape ’from
becoming a tremendous potentate in N.S. Wales.
He and Ovens, whose name is dabbed on a score
of geographical features of three Australian
Provinces, were brother-subalterns, and fought
through the Peninsular War. On their regiment
being ordered, however, to Botany Bay, Which-
cote exchanged, preferring the gory battle-field to
being boss-constable and founder of a first family in
thepenalColonies. Ovens, when chief-engineer, and
therefore having nominal control over the surveys
of the time, promised, in ’23 or ’24. a big grant to
Whichcote “in token of their long friendship.”
But, as Whichcote didn’t come, it is presumed
5000 acres or so of N.S.W. territory kicked the
beam when weighed against the prospect of being
shot through the head for the honour of his king
and the glory of his bleeding country. In a map
of the Twenties, one of the Blue Mountain Peaks
is entitled, “ Wicheote’’—obviously after the
chief-engineer’s comrade-in-arms. Ovens is buried
at St. Leonards’ Cemetery, North Sydney, in the
tomb of his friend, Judge Ellis Bent, but his
remains were originally interred with Bent’s on
Garden Island, Sydney Harbour. One of Ovens’
playful little characteristics has already afforded
“Price Waning” a subject for a story, but the
sequel to that narrative, which would have to
relate the circumstances attendant on the removal
of the remains, while well-known to some persons
m Sydney, could not be published except in a
very much Bowdlerised condition. It was Ovens
by the way, who confirmed, if fie did not
originate, the use of the appellation of “ Vittoria”
for the celebrated N.S.W. Biue .Mountains
eminence, now termed Mt. “ Victoria?’—Vittoria
having been one of the battles at which Ovens
was present. In any case, the evidence is
undoubted that “ Mt. Victoria,” as a topograhi-
cal designation, was first employed by Major
Mitchell, and that simply as a compliment in
anticipation to Her Pre - Eminent -in - Grief
Majesty.
The guardian angel of her helpless sex,
Whom no fatigue could daunt, no crosses vex j
With manly reason and with spirit pure,
Crowned with the blessings of the grateful poor—

Altho’ G. R. Dibbs is the first avowedly
Republican Australian Premier, Adye Douglas
(las.) win the first avowed Republican who
became a Premier in Australasia. Dibbs has been
the father of many children, while Douglas has
been the husband -of many wives. Mrs. Douglas
is the last of four beautiful sisters whom the
gallant Adye lias led to the altar. The Qilgflfl
would not allow Mrs. Douglas to be presented at
Court, when Adye was Agent General for Tas-
mania, because marriage with a deceased wife's
sister was not the correct thing in England.
* * *
t An investigation of the records of the
N.S.W. Lands titles Office would cause some
people to fly an Australian province. Here is one
little job, one of many, probably, perpetrated by
uhe managing director of an alleged investment
company. Mr. John Smith, a Victorian, owned
some lots near the metropolis of an adjoining
province. In an inflated market the lots were
worth £800; and at that figure he made an offer
of them to Managing Director Brown. “Well,’*
suggested the Official, “suppose t get you £150<3
for the lots, will you give me £500?” “Oh,
yes,’ said Mr. Smith ; and in a short time the
directorate accepted the lots at the fancy figure,
and Mr. Smith and Mr. Brown were well satisfied
with their share in the transaction.

That little incident before the N.S.W.
I till Court, the other day, when two prominent
counsel held briefs for the same side through a
solicitor’s error—recalls the fact that on one
occasion, during a busy time, the late “Dick”
Ireland, Q.C , rushed from Redmond Larry's
‘new court” to Sir William StawelTs “old
court, ’ and commenced addressing the jury as for
the plaintiff while he was actually briefed for the
defendant. He had got well under weigh when
liis junior—who happened also to be his son-in-
law—plucked him by the gown and whispered
him. Old Dick got out of the trouble very
adroitly. “That, gentlemen,” said he, “is what
the learned counsel for plaintiff will tell you in
reply to my case for the defendant.” &c &c

The “man Yardley,” who, according to
Mr. Fitzgerald, M.L.A., “stood at Sir Saul
Samuel’s elbow ” when he refused to apply for a
passport for the N.S.W. labour-delegate, is a
short, bald-headed potentate who looks like a
Methodist preacher. He was formerly a clerk in
the Victorian Agent-General’s office, and obtained
his present appointment by mere accident. Capt.
Jopp, who was the secretary to the N.S.W. Agent-
General, having been offered an Imperial a| point-
men t, asked William Forster’s permission to
retire. Forster pointed out that his sudden resig-
nation would be most inconvenient, when Jopp
said he knew a man who would suit him. “Bring
him to me,” said Forster, and accordingly Yard”
ley was ushered in and appointed at once. When
he entered the office he knew absolutely nothing
of the country which was to pay his salary, and
his knowledge even now doesn’t amount to much.
He has never been in any of the Anstralias (this
remark applies to all the other officials of the
N.S.W. Agency-General save the chief, Sir Saul),
nor does he know anything about the people.’
And this is the man who. in many respects is
virtually Agent-General. By the way, Sir Saul
Samuel managed to have ail his staff put upon the
N.S.W. Civil Service list. They therefore hold
their positions during good behaviour, and are
entitled to pensions or compensation for loss of
office. In this respect their positions differ from
those of the staffs of any of the other Agents-
General. Which shows how narrowly Parliamen-
tary votes should be watched.

There was tribulation at Sydney Trades
Hall the other night. Over 100 members of
duiirtcii Were discussing some grave question
wlmri twd loudly-dressed and highly-excited
females presented themselves at the door alld
demanded to see—well —Mr. Simon Tappertit, afi
aspirant for Parliament. The janitor said Sim
was not there, but the ladies were incredulous.
1 hey retired to the street and reviled Sim. in
language unfit for publication, in which they
contrasted his professions as a Sunday-school
teacher with his performances as a Lothario.
I hen the fair ones retreated, promising to return
with reinforcements at the next meeting night.
I hey kept their pledge. Taking advantage of the
doorkeeper’s momentary absence, four of them
burst, into Labour’s Parliament, and gave Simon
a series of homilies, based on the one-man-one-
woman doctrine, to which Simon listehed, stnok-
mg, with that apparent equanimity which is the
proper policy for such an occasion. Forced to
tlie door, these indignant females recounted the
alleged wrongs of one of their number to a sym-
pathetic member, who counselled them, if they
wished to bring matters to a climax, to smash a
window get “lumbered ” and plead justi-
fioatii n. They then left, breathing vengeance and
threatening a new plan of campaign for the next
night.

-Ihe writer once purchased 100 shares from a
godly stockbroker at 15s, per share, and after-
wards dined with that shining light en fami'lc.

The “Women’s Crusade” is a romantic
little boil on the neck of the “Sydney Rescue
Work Society,” and one of its most fascinating
channels of labour is the visitation of various city
and suburban lock-ups on Sunday mornings
and ladies armed with gruesome pails of
slop tea and throat-tickling piles of bread-
and-butter. At a festive celebration, the
other day, graced by (amongst other cele-
brities) G. E. Ardill and the Rev. Soo
Moo Ten—who, though a Chinese, is by far the
most English-gentlemanly-looking cleric in the
Anglican diocese of Sydney—the mouthpiece of
the Crusade related that, during the past five
months* 282 female and 808 male drunks had been
provided with Sunday-morning cups of tea and
bread-and butter in oiled
O, woman, in onr sober houf,
Tll3' little finger makes us cower ;
But when our head’s the size of three,
Whv taunt us with a cup of tea?
Can fancy paint the feelings, utter,
"iou mock with slabs of bread-and-butter ?
Avaunt! and send along the har-gal,
She knows the regulation gargle.
Fancy the frosty morality of a ministering tor-
mentress who prescribes tea and greased bread
for alcoholic dyspepsia and a stamper-battery in
the brain ! r lhe Lady Crusaders must amend
uieir programme in the interests of solid reform.
They must first instil placidity of stomach before
requiring complacency of mind, Absinthe is
highly recommended as a pick-me-up. Worcester-
shire sauce, with the yolk of an egg beaten up, is
a simple Yankee remedy. A deftlv-compounded
ammonia mixture is not to be sneezed at. Tim tin e
of capsicum in cinnamon- water, sweetened with
sugar, has a rare grip for a callous-throati d
inebriate, and is heavens above tea as a stomachic.
Essence of ginger (strongest tincture) will make
the most hardened drunkard cough up his errors
with gratefulness. All of these are teetotal
allowances, even absinthe, for it is merely worm-
wood. But if the Crusaderesses can stretch a
small point in the direction of (say) rum-and milk,
the Sunday levees will become a popular in-
stitution.
“ Doctor ” Taylor, an individual who has
often figured in “ Scotty the Wrinkler’s ” Bulle-
tin sketches, went to Bonny Dundee some time
ago to receive an inheritance of £70,000; he
returned to Australia a few weeks since with his
money and put up at a Melbourne Coffee Palace.
On Wednesday night, 21st ult., at 10 o’clock, he
went to bed, and next day at two p.rn. a waiter
burst bis room door open and found him on the
floor with a nasty wound on his temple. “ The
Doctor” had had a fit of apoplexy and had lain
where he was found for many hours. He died on
the Thursday night at 0.30 without recovering con-
sciousness. Curiously enough, the waiter who
opened the door had a fit himself immediately
afterwards.

L. L. Smith snorts horribly over the
success of the new Victorian champagne, which
thrives on the negative virtues of “ possessing
lots of fiz and never putting a head on anybody.”
Smith spent years of his valuable life in
mcleavourmg to turn out an innocuous effervescent,
and now the other man is building up a business
un the ruins of his (L.L.’s) experiments. No
wonder he doesn t feel well. Evil-disposed wags
are wearing the baffled doctor to a shadow by
asking him to shout a pint bottle of Blank’s Dry
Imperial. “Can you stand that miserable stuff ?”
screams the young man’s best friend. “No, I
.vant jon to stand it, Smith,” replies the wag—
whereupc'" the snorting begins.

A CARNIVAL IDYLL.
“Did you pull off a pot
On the race ? ”
(I could see he did not
From his face).
“ Did you have a good day ?
I continued to say—
And he tried to look gay
For a space.
Buf his spirit broke down
’Neath the strain,
And his brow wore a frown
Once again ;
Then slowly he spoke,
i wixt a croak and a choke,
“1 am fairly dead-broke,
1 hat is plain.”
I replied, “ Save your greed
Till next Spring,
When we may know, indeed,
A ‘ good thing,’ ”
“ But my cash,” moaned the clerk,
“ Is all wrong - keep it dark—
And I’m off like a lark
On the wing.”
“That’s unless you will lend
Me a bit—
Do a turn for a friend
Who is hit ? ”
Then I trembled a lot,
With my face red and hot,
And, exclaiming “Great Scott! ”
Had a fit.
f i here’s a warrant been drawn
For my chum,
And I stroll on the lawn
Looking glum :
I ve a soft feeling heart
For a pal “in the cart,”
Till he asks me to part—
Then I’m dumb.
Of Course.
The Law must take its course, of course, the Law
must take its course :
’Twill never know remorse, of course, ’twill never
know remorse—
It strings ’em up without a tear, without a pang
or sigh,
And likes to see ’em die, bedad, and likes to see
’em die.
’Tis little use to hang, of course, ’tis little use to
hang
A man while there’s his soul to save, and Harmy
drums to bang ;
But though all decent-thinking folk this doctrine
will endorse,
The Law must take its course, of course, the Law
must take its course.
Misguided Dalton slew his wife, he killed her out
of hand,
And now she has a berth, mavbe, within the
Better Land;
Yet, knowing she is dead and gone, and doing
very well,
The Law decides—we’d better land her hubby
down in hell.
So hither with the gallows-rope and put it on the
beam,
’Tis only when the man’s asleep of mercy he can
dream ;
What matter though his crazy rage has spent its
cruel force ?
Die Law must take its course, of course, the Law
must take its course.
Sing hey the pulling of the bolt, the “horrid
r swish of blood,”
The “trembling wretch” who has to fall and
make a “ sick’ning thud ” ;
Let common-sense in vain protest, and scream
until it’s hoarse—
So long as there’s a man to hang the Law will
take its course.

Old John Cox, of Miingoplah, was the
most popular man in the Wage a district, for his
hand was ever in his pocket, and he was always
ready to do a good turn to anybody lie liked.
Amongst others indebted to him for kindnesses
was Pat. % the brewer. Some time after
Cox’s death Pat. came to Wagga at Circuit time,
and on his way back to Albury be was accom-
panied by a certain barrister and a Crown law
official. They were travelling by a back track to
Germanton, and on the way they had to pass the
Wagga cemetery, where poor Cox’s bones lay.
“Stop,” said Pat., who was pretty well “on,”
“we must say a prayer over poor John Cox’s
grave.” Accordingly, the trio dismounted from
the buggy, and, after search, the grave was found.
“Do any of yez know the day yrofundis?” en-
quired Pat. “ I’ll thry,” said the barrister, who
proceeded to repeat the psalm in Latin, but, after
a line or two, broke down. Nor was the Crown
law official a bit more successful in English. At
last Pat. lost patience, and said : “ Shtop ; I’ll say
a prayer meself. God bless you, John Cox, wherever
you arc I' 1

Amongst seme historical relies destroyed
in a Tasmanian fire last month were a gold watch
which belonged to Governor Collins, and the
desk upon which Chief Justice Tedder used to
draft his decisions. The owner of the relics, of
course, was an octogenarian. The island is full
of octogenarians.

A sherill’s-oflber at Wagga, N.S.W., has
just celebrated his “ golden wedding.”
Curious how some men who ought to have
been making the grass grow green a generation
ago survive I Thomas Henry Braim, the" alleged
Doctor of Divinity who was head master or the"
Church Grammar School, Hobart Town, from
1835 to ’4l, and Principal of Sydney College from
'll to’4B, died in England a week, or two ago.
His chi f claim to be remembered is that be wrote
a History.of New South Wales, which was pub-
lished in two volumes, in 1846. As a combinaticn
of the.sermon and the guide-book the work is not
without merit, but to the students of this genera-
tion it is rendered valueless by the too obvious
desire of the author to paint a very rotten state of
things in tints of rosy-red. He was a strong
supporter of the powers that were, and it was due
to hii out-and-out resolve to holster up .Bishop
Broughton’s ecclesiastical pretentions that he was
admitted to holy orders, which he took on
returning to England, lie came out again to
Australia and was pitchforked by Bishop Perry, of
Melbourne, into the Archdeaconry of Portland
(Western Yic.), much against the wishes of the
Hentys, the sturdy old pioneers of Merino Downs
Station, who were almost as good churchmen as
they were squatters. Frank Henry wanted the
Bishop to appoint that notable clergyman and
splendid bushinan, Doctor Cusack Rus ell, “The
Bishop of the West,” whose memory is revered
to-day even by men who never enter a Church.
Braim retired from the Archdeaconry in
1866, as “ being incapable of further ministerial
work,” finally went “home,” and, apparently,
because of the incapacity aforesaid, held several
wealthy rectorships, whose souls he cared for by
means of curates on £BO a year or so.
» # #
“Price Waning,” by the way, has a letter
of Braim’s which is interesting as showing the
surpassing generosity of the man. It is so charac-
teristic of the clerical temperament to help others
along in the world by invoking the aid of friends.
Braim was not a cleric when he wrote the letter,
but he was qualifying rapidly. Here’s the
screed :
Sydney, September 30, 1843.—'Siv Dear Sir,—lt is out
of my power at this moment to render you any pecuniary
assistance- can you not obtain it from your Lodge? If I
were in a position to help you I would gladly. Mr. Sat-
terie did speak to mo about printing, and in the event of
his being able among bis friends to raise the money to
print his lectures (on Education), I told him he would
lind Mr. Bent the cheapest man and one who deserved
encouragement. Mr. S has some good friends who were
(or are) expected to help him in the publishing of his
work. I am very willing to throw in my mite for the
good of any friend, and especially for one united to me by
the “mystic tye.”—Yours truly, T. K. Braim.
. The letter is written to Andrew Bent—-the “ Aus-
tralian Franklin,” the “ father of the Tasmanian
Press”—titles given tohim by Edward Smith Hall.
Thanks to the press policy of Governor Arthur
and Solicitor-General Alfred Stephen, Bent, after
20 years of fighting for freedom of speech in the
Southern Island, was financially crushed and
came to Sydney, where he made an appeal for
assistance to start a printing business. Braim
replied in the manner indicated. Sir Alfred
Stephen, however, subscribed £5 to Bent’s assist-
ance, “notwithstanding,” as he wrote, “that
Mr. Bent said very many unkind tilings about
me when I was in Van Dieman’s Land.” Old
Bent’s necessities (so he noted upon the Judge’s
letter) prevented him returning the fiver,

A Brisbane firm of undertakers has gone
insolvent through “depression in trade.” The
fact is that people can't afford to die in Banana-
land just now.
# # #
“Five Years a Waitress” writes to The
Bulletin a long letter of which this paragraph is
the central point:—
The meanness of Man in liis business dealings with
Woman was never better shown than in the fact that
when a man gets his dinner at a restaurant provided with
male waiters, he almost invariably “lips” those male
waiters; whereas when he buys his meal at a restaurant
where women serve, he refrains from uiving any tip. So
general is this rule that whereas most waiters receive
handsome additions to their wages through the source
mentioned, it can lie boldly said that a waitress-I mean,
of course, the honest waitress—doesn’t make a shilling a
week in gifts.

If you ever see Captain “Chumley,”
A.D.C., with a very large black eye and his jaw
in a sling, you must not think he has been
fighting. The young man has only been playing
polo.

In a N.S W. Government department
lately, a controversy took place as to the spelling
of a certain word. Six feet four of the imported
British article silenced the wranglers by spelling
the word incorrectly, and then affirming, “ I have
an innate knowledge both of spelling and eti-
quette.”
» # *
The chief hindrance to the extension of
trades-unionism among women is the chance of
marriage. Every working-woman learns a trade
merely as an expediency, for marriage is her goal ;
but the working-man espouses his trade for life,
marriage or no marriage.

They wanted Coroner Yolil to hold an
inquest on a skull last week, but he wouldn’t.
There wasn’t enough of it.
* * *
The latest Sydney connubial scandal
proves, if proof were needed, how foolish it is for
a girl to marry a man who is too old for company
and too young to die.
# * *
This tale is about two brothers who went
over to Maoriland and bought a farm. They
were a hard-working pair, and upright in all their
dealings, but one of them smoked, and the other
didn’t. Smoking is a low and beastly habit,
as may be ascertained by consulting the good
little Sunday-school b:ok about the small boy
who was always pious and who died of consump-
tion when he was very young. The individual
who didn’t smoke put away the money thus saved,
and when he had got £lO together he bought a
valuable cow with the loot. The cow cost £2O, so
he went into debt for the other £lO. Then as he
was getting the animal home it bolted into a
quarry and broke its neck, and its owner was £lO
worse off than if he had smoked all his life. He
got back to the farm in a solemn mood, and
found that the other fellow had set the house on
fire while lighting his pipe, and it had been burnt
to the ground. If the one brother hadn’t smoked
he wouldn’t have set the house on fire, and if the
other one had smoked he wouldn’t have been
away fooling with the cow, and would have been
on hand to extinguish the fire. Anybody who
wants to get up an anti tobacco argument can sort
these facts out at his leisure.

It is all but impossible to get any enquiry
instituted into the Bank of V.D.L. affairs in con-
sequence of so many being “in it.” Leading
lawyers and—er—avenues of public opinion are
gagged on account of their own overdrafts. The
ast idea is to get a smart Melbourne legal firm to
take the matter up in the interests of the wretched
shareholders. A Judge like Wrenfordsley is
needed to hear the case.
# * *
Goldsmith, of the Melbourne bar, who
appeared at Hobart recently in a big property
dispute case, told Henry Dobson, the Tasmanian
junior, who opened on the other side, that he
(Dobson) had treated the Court to “ three days’
idiotic drivel.” So much for the amenities of the
profession!
# * *
Melbourne has its chancery suit on hand,
and it has lasted six years, so that two Judges
concerned, and both the leading lawyers, have
died.

There are 1047 churches and chapels in
S. Australia, or one for every 303 of the popula-
tion —exclusive of the saints at present in gaol.

* * #
A Sydney bank official the other day gave
it as his opinion that men who stayed away from
the office through influenza were loafers—he
would never allow such a paltry ailment to stop his
work. Two days afterwards he was laid up, and,
judging from the latest bulletins, his funeral will
probably be about next Sunday afternoon.
* * “ #
The cable tells us that the husband of
Madame Melba, the Australian prirna donna,
seeks to divorce her on account of her alleged
alultery with the Duke of Orleans, from whom
£20,000 damages are claimed. It also says that
Melba declares the divorce-action to be mere
retaliation for a petition filed by her for judicial
separation, on the ground of cruelty. Very likely
the world will never ascertain the real merits of
this interesting case, which once more illustrates
the broad generality, that there can be no happi-
ness in marriage between an actress and a man
who is not an actor - .
Mr. John Smedley, late of Sydney, and
well-known there as an architect and artist, has
settled in Yokohama, Japan, in which country he
resided years ago.

Two rusty pistols were lately found in the
bush near Wyagdon Hill (N.S.W.), which are
supposed to have some connection with the
murder of Constable Codrington in 1857, the per-
petrators of which were never discovered. Cod-
rington’s widow has, since her husband’s death,
drawn from a generous Government one shilling
per day as compensation for the loss of her
husband !
Mr. Charles A. B. Moody writes from
21 Battersea Rise, Claphain Junction, London,
S.W., entreating us to ascertain for him and his
wi e, who are advanced in years, the whereabouts
of their son. John James Moody, who left Eng-
land in 1883 as a steward on K.M.S. Orient, and
last wrote home in 1888, when about to become
skating-instructor at Skinner and Ridgley's Exhi-
bition Rink, Brisbane, saying he expected to be
in England in June or July, 1888, and would
write on arriving at Plymouth. He was to bring
with him a companion named Robert Hughes, but
up to now nothing more has been heard of either.
We shall be glad if any reader will send news.
Writes a Far Northern correspondent :
.It is a curious fact noted by stocluneu that old bulls
hunted out of herds of either wild or tame cattle
invariably make for the east in the Slates ; old buffalo
1 mils always make for the foot hills of the Rockies when
hunted away from the droves; hut in Australia the
buffalo outcasts follow the rule of their scarcely less wild
brethren and make tor the east. A buffalo hull has been
killed as far east as Lolworth Station, close to Charters
Towers, Q., and at a station near Herberton one was
killed by Mr. Wallace Grant and party last year. Into
■.las brute not less than sixteen bullets from a Snider rifle
,verc fired, and even then the hunters had to knock him
on the head with an axe. The chase lasted for miles, but
Mr. Grant, an expert stockman, kept “working” the
brute in a circle, and finally got him close to the station-
house, where the chase ended. Those who have never
seen the buffalo would not believe how thick the hide is
over the neck and on the frontlet. There are few, if any,
rifles which would penetrate the adamantine wall that
protected the brain of the animal killed at Herberton.
The horns are not slender and sharp-pointed, as shown in
an engraving in a somewhat celebrated work recently
published in Sydney, on the contrary, they are exceed-
ingly thick at the base and taper but little towards the
points, which are very blunt.
What sort of iron man is this ?
The spectacle is numbing,
Full 50 years of married bliss,
Full 25 of “ bumming
No matter who salt tears are shedding,
He celebrates his “ golden wedding.”
What “executions ” he has notched
With earmarks faintly valid 1
How many widows’ houses watched
And orphans’ playthings tallied !
Fate’s victims have a right to rail if
Old Time deals gently with a bailiff.
The sight of misery can't kill,
For habitude makes callous;
The same as vice will flourish still
Beneath the very gallows.
The man sees numberless to-morrows
Who runs the rule o’er others’ sorrows.
Our joker was a bank-clerk first;
Then, digger; next, book-keeper;
Then, ran a store; then, last and vyorct,
A bailiff. Say, some weeper,
Can there be any Kingdom-come
For quarter-een ury of bum?

TITUS SALT.
A Sydney building society is drawing a
rental of £3 a-week from premises mortgaged for
£15,000!
Some Sydney publicans’ names—Bacchus,
Sucker, Kickham, Philpots, Negus, Beer, Grand-
fill, Brewer, Paine.
Napoleon said the Duchess of Angouleme
“’was the only man among the Bourbons.”
Where is the man among the Guelphs?
Jews and guinea-pigs never catch cholera.
Diabetes and shortsightedness are the ailments to
which the former are most generally subject. To
these may also be added —shortsighted promissory-
notes.
Many men in the North bear marks of
desperate encounters with the niggers, but it is
doubtful if anyone there carries such terrible
sears as does Jack Kennedy, now a blacksmith at
Hughenden, N.Q. ; certain it is that few men
recover from brain injuries so extensive as he
received. In the desperate fight which marked
the man for life and in which he received wounds
which would have killed any two ordinary men,
he won against big odds. One of Kennedy’s mates
tells to The Bulletin the story of the row. The
party to which K. belonged owned a claim some
distance from Port Darwin, and were engaged in
sinking a shaft thereon. One day Kennedy took
a team of horses and, in company with a fourteen-
year-old boy, started out to bring in timber for
the shaft. After going some miles, the horses
were unhooked, and, having lunched, Kennedy
took the traces off a heavy pair of brass hames in
order to do some repairs; having attended to
which matter, he lay down beneath the dray. He
had slept but a few moments, when he was
aroused by the boy yelling: “Niggers, Jack,
niggers!” Grasping one of the hames, K. darted
out from under the dray, but was met, as he started
to his feet by a blow from a tomahawk which split
open his forehead and filled his eyes with blood.
Sweeping the blood away with his left hand,
Kennedy began to swing his hame, and for the
next few moments there was as merry a little
fight as has ever been seen in the Territory.
While the shindy was progressing the boy under
the dray kept yelling, “Give ’em hell, Jack-
give ’em hell.” And Jack did “ give ’em hell,”
for he killed three of those niggers, and so badly
wounded others, that they had to retreat. After
the battle Kennedy fainted from loss of blood,
but was brought round by the boy, who finally
got him into the dray, hitched up a pair of the
horses and started back for camp, which was
reached with the wounded man more dead than
alive. However, be recovered, and is to-day as
perfect a picture of manhood as there is in
Australia. From the blow in the back of the
head he has a depression four inches long, an
inch-and-a-quarter wide, and over half-an-inch
deep. His left cheek is disfigured by a dreadful
scar five inches long, the cut making which went
to the bone and knocked out several teeth. In
addition there is a terrible scar from the first
blow over the right eye, and numerous others on
neck, face, shoulders, and breast. Kennedy does
not blow about the fight.

We would respectfully refer as to our reliability to the Editor of this paper.
Let it be distinctly understood that this is no
scheme of quackery to draw money out of the
afflicted, nor is it any sort of a patent medicine
humbug, since there is not a grain of medicine of any
kind required in the treatment. Notwithstanding
this fact, it takes directly hold of the worst cases
of constipation, dyspepsia, liver complaints, head-
aches, heart disease, incipient consumption,
diabetes, and Bright’s disease of the kidneys, in-
cluding fevers and inflammation of the lungs, and
of other internal organs—not by attacking these
so-called diseases directly, but by radically neutra-
lising and removing their causes, thus allowing
nature herself to do her work unimpeded.
We now simply repeat that the new treatment
acts so radically and fundamentally upon the vital
circulation without the least danger to the most
delicate constitution, that there is not a form of
disease that flesh is heir to, which will not be
affected favorably to the patient by an intelligent
use of this method of treatment.
In selling this Pamphlet we are obliged to re-
fequire a “Pledge of Honor” from those purchas-

of their own families. This is for our own protec-
tion, and as the only means by which our just
rights in the discovery can be maintained.
Boctors, of course, who purchase are not debarred
from using the treatment in their own practice,

DR. R. F. STEVENS’ INDORSEMENT.
Dr. Stevens, of Syracuse, N.Y., was the first
M.D. to whom we revealed the new treatment —
more than 20 years ago. In writing to a friend,
about a year ago, he gives the following :
***** “Nearly twenty years ago in this city Dr. A.
Wilford Hall gave me privately a detailed history of a
hygienic treatment he had discovered and adopted some
twenty years before, and which he had practised con-
tinuously upon himself up to that time. The history
included an account of the failure of the health, the
reasonings and conclusions which led him to adopt the
treatment, his rapid restoration to health, &c. From the
time of his first mention of it to me I have been entirely
satisfied that it saved him from a lingering consumption
and death ; and that in the years following, as I have evi-
dence to satisfy me, its continued practice has so affected
the nutrient and eliminating functions as to give to his
physical structure a greater degree of solidity, or, in
other words, a higher specific gravity than that possessed
by any other man living. 1 learn that he has persistently
continued the treatment to the present time - forty years
in all—and that his present health and vigorous condition
may rightfully be compared to the ‘bloom of youth,’
though ho is nearly seventy years of age. The tests I
have made of the treatment upon myself and in my prac-
tice in the twenty years past, have convinced me that
great practical benefit may be derived by members of the
medical profession and others from a careful study of the
rationale of the treatment as discovered by Dr. Hall, the
details of which he proposes to give in his forthcoming
book. A debt of gratitude will surely he due to him for
whatever scientific advances he may bo able to unfold in
the lino of assisting nature to ward off disease, and in the
restoration of health.—ll. F. Stevens, M.D.”
“ Sydney, 11th August, 1891.
“ Mr. J. C. Oakman.—Dear Sir,—l have great pleasure
in recommending Dr. A. Wilford Hall’s treatment, as I
have derived great benefit from its use. I have for years
been suffering from torpid liver, dyspepsia, constipation,
and the last two years from inward piles ; but after the
use of Dr. A. W. Hall's treatment for about two months
I find myself entirely cured of that very painful com-
plaint the piles, and all my other troubles are getting
gradually less, and I firmly believe that a continuation of
this treatment will restore me to perfect health again. I
shall be very happy to give any one any further informa-
tion if deserved.—Yours very truly, Robert Tarin, 231
Goulburn-street, Surry Hills.”
“ Wellshot Station, Ist August, 1891.
“ Mr. J. C. Oakman, 8 O Connell-street, Sydney. —Dear
Sir, —Yours of July 14 to hand. Thank you very much
for your kind offer, to help me in case I could not under-
stand thoroughly the pamphlet or if I found difficulty in
applying the treatment. It is not the case. I understand
English as well as French, and as for the treatment it has
already done a world of good, and the pamphlet is worth
hundred times the money. If it had been translated in
French 1 would have advised some friends and
relations at home to buy it. This is the
reason why I was asking, and I hope it will soon
be translated in every known language and spread
all over the world. I have been suffering from dyspepsia
for the last 20 years, and a few days of this treatment
has done me more good and given me more relief than
all the remedies I ever tried before.—l am, dear sir, yours
aithfully, Jude Derbbxv.”
The distinguished pulpit-orator and evangelist,
Rev. Miles Grant, Boston, Mass., writes,
October 7 :
“ ***Touching your wonderful discovery, I take much
pleasure in saying I am delighted with its effects upon
my system. I sent for it that I might know its influence
on a healthy person. I have now used it about three
months, and intend to continue its use during my mortal
life. The treatment produces a general refreshing, in-
vigorating effect: sharpens my appetite, sweetens my
sleep, and makes me feel as thougli I had a new lease of
life. 1 have persuaded several of my brethren of the
ministry to send for your Health-Pamphlet, and so far as
1 have heard from them they are exceedingly pleased.
One of them said to me a few days ago, • I would not
take $5O for it. It knocks the bottom out of disease.’ I
can say for myself I never spent cash to better advantage
than when 1 bought your pamphlet —Your brother in
Christ, Miles Grant.”
“ Sydney, June S, 1891.

month the treatment described in Dr. A. W. Hall's

improvement in health, so great that 1 cannot describe
how very different my feelings are, for life is now enjoy-
able; but for nine years past it has often been veiy

paralysis of tongue, and other parts; also frequent

to Dr. Hall, and yourself, that I am so well, for a month
ago I felt as if some dreadful climax was near.—Yours
truly, Susan E. Hawkins, 58 Henry-street, Leichhardt,
N.S.W.”
“ Glenburn Public School, Wiseman’s Creek,

testifyin\ to the efficiency of Dr. Hall's hygienic treatment.
I have bum a sufferer from indigestion and constipation
in its woivt forms for the last eight years, and during

have not felt any pain, and can sleep quite sound every
night. I have not felt the slightest touch of indigestion
since, and can eat three hearty, good meals every day.
Everybody says how well I begin to look. My wife arid

stomach, liver, kidney, and bowels.—Faithfully yours,
