The “Boomer.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
York Peninsula (N.Q.) 20 years ago. A
wilderness of niggers, heat, snakes, alligators,
fever-and-ague, hunger, and men of angry passions
and skill with firearms. Altogether an insanitary
country, especially that around the B : g Mitchell.
Yet men, fired with the gold-fever, flocked thither
from all directions —from Ballarat and Bendigo,
from Hokitika and Hill End, from anywhere and
everywhere between Shark Bay and Point Danger.
Some are there still—underground.
Among the pioneers of 20 years ago was an
enterprising individual who amassed much wealth
in a short time, without undergoing any of the
fatigue of digging for it. This was “The
Boomer,” known by that title for a hundred
miles round, and admittedly the smartest
horse-thief within 150 miles of Cooktown.
He was a big fellow, and supposed to be
desperately savage ; mounted police were scarce,
and not too anxious to meet such an expert with
the revolver as, he was reputed to be. So “The
Boomer” continued gaily on his career of annexa-
tion —stole the diggers’ horses with the strictest
impartiality, and by close attention to business
flourished and throve exceedingly. He was a born
raider; in fact, his talent for the profession
amounted to genius; and, if it had not been for
one unfortunate weakness in his character, lie
might have continued his lucrative employment to
this day. But he had one serious defect in his
disposition ; he was too theatrical in his methods
—too fond of posing, as it were. He would risk
capture through a feverish love of approbation
which more than once caused him to run off a mob
of horses through the midst of a diggers’ camp in
broad daylight. Or, if in the course of business,
he lifted an unusually good horse, he, wouldn’t dis-
pose of it along with the rest of his haul. He’d
ride it about in a calm and unconcerned, and
almost obtrusive manner. It was very showy, but
it wasn’t good business.
* * *
One day the mailman called at the camp
on the Big Mitchell on his way to Cooktown. He
hobbled his saddle-horse and his pack-horse and
turned them loose for “an hour or two.” But
when he went to get them, his riding-horse wasn’t
there. And before evening a man arrived who
had met that identical prad about three miles out,
“ goin’ along nice and easy,” he said, “under the
command of a big, black-whiskered cove, with
white pants and a cabbage-tree hat.”
“It’s that there ‘Boomer’ again, Dan,” said a
sympathiser.
“ Well,” said Dan, “I’m after him ! And if I
catch him, I’ll ‘Boomer ’ him, by Heavens, I will!”
Dan also made some more remarks, chiefly of a
profane nature. Then, leaving the mailbags
behind, pro tern,, he mounted his pack-horse, and
rode off in the direction indicated by the man who
had last seen Dan’s property.
* * *
The mailman returned about daylight next
morning. He was riding the big brown saddle-
horse, and leading the pack-horse upon which he
had ridden away. He said he had found the horse
a few miles away, hobbled ; it had only just
strayed away. “That fellow that saw a man on
his back must ha’ bin drunk,” said Dan. “The
bloomin’ horse wasn’t stole at all!”
“Where’s the hobbles, Dan?” said someone, as
Dan dismounted.
Dan gave a start. “Oh ! the hobbles ! Why,
1 -didn’t I bring ’em? 1 must ha’ left cm on
the ground when 1 caught the horse. 15r——.”
Just then another inqirsitive bystander chipped
i n—
“ Where did you raise the halter, Dan, to lead
the old pack-horse back? You went away without
one, ’cause I saw you I ’
“ Pity you didn’t have time to mind your own
business ! ’ said Dan.
“But where did you get it, Dan? I want to
know, ’cause it looks bloomin’ like a baiter that
useter belong .”
“Eellcr lent it me,” growled Dan; “’taint
yours, nohow !”
And he packed his mail-bags, climbed on his
horse, and departed.
# * *
A couple of mounted troopers happened to
ride a’ong that way a few days later. As they
crossed the dried-up, gravelly bed of a small
creek, their horses simultaneously snorted and
shied. The men swore at the horses and then
looked round to see—“ What the something you’re
frightened at?”
They found it. If the flies had not been making
as much noise as a hive of bees they would have
discovered “it” in another way. North Queens-
land summer weather has a very powerful effect
on such things.
Stretched at full length amid the long grass
was the dead body of a black-bearded man of
large size. There was a small hole in the right
side of his forehead, which, however, couldn’t be
seen till a layer of flies, an inch deep, had been
shifted. There were ants, too, and other creeping
things, and
The troopers 1 held a short consultation —at a
distance.
“ Wish to goodness we’d come some other way,
Jack. A wise man never found a dead man.”
“ Who’s to say we didn’t ? ”
“ Will you, though ? ”
“ Rather ! There’s no track near this p’ace.
Mightn't ever be anybody along here again for
years.”
“ Well, by George, I’m with you ! I’d rather
lose five pound than have the handling of that.
I wish we had a nip ! Let’s make for old Skinner’s
shanty ! ”
So they left.
And since then no man has ever set eyes on
“ The Boomer.”
A.C.

It was dry work a field parade in November,
and Stubbs’, genialest of military secretaries, was

In short, there Was in the unk - wainscotted

with her squill anus that catch, and monster-guns
that hurst. Good fellows they were—here and

men fitly chosen to officer companies composed of
tlie essence of those adventurous souls who made
up that glorious inrush into Victoria of the Fifties
and Sixties.
Ah! where are they now? Some survive and
some have gone. Better perhaps for some had the
tide of war, which sometimes seemed so near with
its bloody crest, and yet was always s t far, dashed
upon the virgin Australian beach and swept them
away in its withdrawal. For the ping of a shame-
ful bullet let tlie life out of ’s brain, and the
grim portals of IVntridge closed upon . Others
went to honoured graves, and the triple volleys
have rolled over Stubbs, and Bambers, and
Ashley, after the Masons had dropped on coffin-
lids their evergreens and lambskins. Some have
gone, and others survive—two or three on “re-
tired ” rank to rest—others, alas ! into politics -
one to land-booming, another to save the wreckage
of the boom, and a third—saddest fate of all!—to
become an Agent-General.
* • •
As the great pedestal clock—a legacy from the
old 14th (2nd)—“ the rowdiest, most disreputable
battalion that ever disgraced us with their pre-
sence in our midst,” said the loyal Argus—rang
out six o’clock there was a change in the hearty,
laughterful noise.
The Military Secretary rose and drew his belt
in. “ Sorry, gentlemen, I’ve to leave you, but
his voice dropped a tone—“l always dine with
the—the—Colonel—on Saturday nights. But call
for what you want, gentlemen !”
There was a clatter of scabbards on the floor
and a clash of glasses on the table as the officers
rose. Than those noises not a sound; scarcely a
breathing, for, perhaps, a half-minute. Then—
“I give the Colonel-the dear old Colonel,
comrades !” cried Draven.
The Colonel-Coin maud ant, gentlemen!” .Stubbs
repeated.
“ The Colonel-Commandant !“ they said one by
one, as the glasses went up to lips—slowly. It
was curious how slowly !
W him the glasses were down, the Military
Secretary spoke again. They had looked one at
another and all at him, and he answered the
looks.
“Yes, gentlemen-comrades-let us - let us—
give him musical honours ! It may be ri'l be —
the lust t me !”
Then they looked at Balkan, of the ©oilingwood.
Balkan was a singing-master. Often and often
had his full baritone sot the key fur camp song
and mess-room chorus.
But the devil was in the wine or something.
No public-school youngster had ever been rapped
over tlie head by Pallan's baton for emitting a
falser, wretcheder note than that which now
whistled, gasped, struggled through Pallan’s own
lips. The officers would have roared with laughter
if they had not been so near—the other tiling.
Then Softgoode tried. He was a member of a
Congregational Church choir, and it was scarcely
cor ect conduct in him to strike up a drinking-
chorus, though joining in one might be permis-
sible. He tried twice, and the third time a bass
and a tenor helped him with the repetition of—
For he’s a jolly good fellow !
while a voice t hat was not classifiable nor recognis-
able, so strident was it, joined at the—
And so say all of us !
As for the others, they were silent. Three or
four Ivad turned their faces to the wall at the poor,
piteous attempt to add the musical honours to the
toast, and, one stared vacantly in the eyes of
Captain Stubbs’s servant. The vocal salute was a
failure.
But their Commandant should not be deprived
of some honour with the toast.
“ Comrades ! ” murmured Stubbs : “ wo can't
sing ! But—there I ” He snapped his wine-glass,
and threw stem and bowl on the table, where they
shattered.
One by one the others followed his example.
As the last cup shivered into atoms, Ashley, of
the Richmond, said, hoarsely: “Good God !
What a health-drinking ! ”
“ Health ! We’d have drunk to his health only
"’hen we’d drunk to his death!” responded
Draven.
For the Colonel was dying a death that appalled
even these strong men.
* * #
Without word of command, without, in-
deed, any word, but moved by mute impulse and
a spontaneous regard, the officers tightened their
belts, replaced shakos and busbies, and formed
into line. The junior subaltern was nearest the
door opening into the men’s dormitory overlooking
St. Kilda Road. The shadow of tlie huge blue-
stone buildings, thrown by the refulgence of a sun
that was setting in an aureole of magnificence, lay
pall-like on the ground.
“Right-face—march.” Stubbs, by virtue of his
Imperial rank, gave the order, and, through the
dormitory the eighteen or twenty officers filed.
At the outer lintel they formed into twos, and
by twos they passed out—into the shadow. The
brilliant gleams of the lowering sun fell upon
them gloriously when they had emerged from the
gloominess, and drew a thousand sparkles from
belt-buckles and sword-knots, and steely scab-
bards. More than one of that small company saw
an a’legory in that brief passage from shade into
light,
Twenty minutes their march lasted. It was too
long for some; too short for others. These last
were fearful as they drew near their destination.
There are moments when even a soldier may not
improperly indulge in a feeling of dread. Still,
none fell < ut, and when their tramp ceased at the
bronzed gates in the quiet suburban street and
their scabbards fell for a moment at Stubbs’s
“Halt!” tire wondering people who had been
drawn by the approach of command-less officers,
to verandahs and balconies, were not excited t >
greater wonderment by seeing some remain whilst
otlu rs went away.
# # #
Captain Stubbs alone passed into the gar-
den of the Commandant’s cottage, nor did he
return fur several minutes.
“He will receive you, gentlemen—comrades. If
;iou can bear it, he says he can!” This was the
Military Secretary's message.
“What soy you, gentlemen?” Stubbs ques-
tioned, after a moment’s pause. “Shall you
venture; ?’’
Major Draven answered for his comrades.
“ We are here, sir.”
Captain Stubbs Hung the broad carriage gates
open.
“ Then—forward. By seniors, march!” And,
placing himself at their head, he set the pace.
No one knew how it w-as—none could explain
when, in after days, they talked the incident over
among themselves— but the step he gave them was
that slowest of slow marches which is the fitting
movement of the escort of the dead.
Along gravelled paths, between close-clipped
box-edgings and beds of homely flowers breathing
scents to the balmy air, up stately steps to a
statelier verandah where clustered ferns and
dwarf-pines, and there, again, they paused, form-
ing once more into rank.
“1 should tell you, comrades, that our Colonel
is—in—a darkened room.”
“ We understand,” said Draven.
“No, you do not.”
“ It is quite dark— the least ray of light—natural
or artificial—the flicker of a luoifer-match even, will
cause intense pain—perhaps death. Therefore, I
pray you, be careful. You cannot see him.”
A shudder ran through the rank and the thought
of all was spoken then by one.
“ Then had we not better go away—now ?”
“No, if you can’t sec him, you can speak to
him—so.” He uttered a word, distinctly but in
alow tone. “Not above that!” he went on.
“His hearing is as sensitive to sound as his sight
to light.”
“ But what’s the use ? ”, queried Draven. “ We
can’t see him—we can’t salute him - he will not
know our voices ”
“As you pass beneath the inner curtain,”
Stubbs resumed, “halt. Then utter your name
in a low voice, take three paces, halt again, front,
salute —as quietly as possible, don’t let your arms
jingle—then put out your hand—and you will,
perhaps, after a second’s groping, meet his. Only
a touch, mind you—for your own sakes! —then
return. You understand, gentlemen, all!”
“Yes!” came from some lips. Most were
silent.
‘ * Quietly—and three paces, remember ! By
seniors, now, please. Major Draven, this way !”
From the verandah into a hall, through the
hall where tearful-eyed ladies were grouped,
proud, though, in that terrible moment that their
relative, their hero, should still retain his place in
the hearts of his officers, passed, one by one, the

s
men who had come to do honour to their old
chief. Through the hall, and through a suite of
rooms till the Military Secretary stopped each in
his turn by a curtained doorway.
“ Let fchls curtain fall—open the door—close it
-then pass under the inner curtain.”
And, one by one, they went in,
# * #
_~~.,. . . , . ,
Inside that room, dark as the tomb which its
tenant was so soon to occupy, each officer obeyed
the Military Secretary’s command to the letter,
But the three paces, each confessed afterwards,
were not the firm tread of soldiers, but of feeble
beings horror-stricken as by a stab from the
Unknown.
And this man, isolated in a room which, of
narrow dimensions, was to him a wilderness of
wof, received them with an unstmtedmanhness.
i( graven, of the East Melbourne .
k i k, 3,’ Melb °urne and Carlton .
(( Bmythall, Golluigwood .
Kokes, of the Kew and Hawthorne.
And so on to the junior Lieutenant of the
Southern. io each a simple intense word of
thanks—a momentary touch of the backs of fingers
-but no pressure of palms, for the palm that
should have stretched from the centre of the
apartment, alas ! was not.
To Draven, to Ashley, to another, beyond the
word of thanks there was a muttered inquiry as
to the affairs of their common Masonic Lodge.
“ How is my—old—lodge? Is it well with the
—brethren? Remember me to them all!” And
that he was not forgotten, the minutes of the
next ensuing N. and M. Lodge, No. 1116, E.C.,
testify.
They had all passed into the room to the
youngest subaltern, and had formed once on the
verandah, when a whim seized upon the dying
roan .
“I will see them, Stubbs! Ask them to form
here—the room will hold them all!”
“It will be madness, sir—death !” remonstrated
Stubbs.
“Well, if so, what matter? I’ve borne the
battle long enough—l have faced the enemy—l.
am not deserting my post in taking one step to
meet the relief. Ask them, Stubbs, ask them to
favour their old Colonel again. I crave to look
unon the uniforms once more !”
And though his women, also, prayed him not, he
would have it so. '
They filed in again, noiselessly. The room was
still darkened. But a woman's hand, with in-
stinctive power to do the right thing at the right
moment, drew back the trebly-folded curtains
which shrouded the window, sufficiently to permit
one tiny dart of rose-red splendour to pierce the
rift and send a wavering, quivering line along the
grouped scarlets and deep-blues.
The men m the uniforms saw in the sequence of
that flash that which they never forgot. The tall,
stalwartness of the figure they had once known
was burdened with the decrepitude and the agony
of an awful torture, and the features, which had
once inspired them by its quickening revelations
of a soldier’s soul, masked. Two lurid coals burnt
in the eye-spaces.
The lower part of the mask dropped and the
dying man said: “Wife, more light! I can
bea r ifc ;” ...
Slowly the curtains widened—the grudged light
streamed in boldier and with brighter vividness—
the buttons and the silver-lace glistened like
diamond facets. From a hundred points the rays
of the setting sun were reflected in undimmed
lustre as though they were sudden shafts of an
aurora penetrating Cimmerian night. The hand
which was palmless went up suddenly to the burn-
ing eye-balls,
“ Wife, wife !” shrieked the mask, “ the curtain
—quick—l cannot bear it, I cannot bear it!”
* # »
That night one of the officers told in his
family circle the sad episode of the day; and his son
thoughtover the narrative on thefollowingmorning
when, as was his wont in the early hours of Sun-
day forenoons, he strolled through Studley Park
to take a dip in the Yarra and to enjoy a yarn
with a chum who, by a coincidence, happened
also to be a son of one of the officers who had
shown honour to their colonel on the occasion of
his last levee.
The youths had taken their plunge from the
rocky steps overshadowed by the Victoria-street
bridge, and were preparing for the walk home,
when an old gentleman, grey-headed, fresh-faced,
white-costumed, straw-hatted, who had strolled
down to the bank apparently with the intention
of permitting a water-spaniel to disport himself,
was attracted by their conversation.
“Pop,” said the first lad, “did your governor
tell you of his visit to Colonel ’s yesterday ?”
“Yes, by George, wasn’t that funny?”
“Funny!” exclaimed the other. “It is the
most awful thing I ever heard of. I don’t see
where the fun came in. To think of fine old
Colonel dying in that terrible fashion.”
“What! What! Did you say that Colonel
was dying ? ” The boys turned in amazement, and
then understood that it was the old gentleman
who had interrupted them.
Wonderingly they replied. The lad who had
first spoken answered, “Yes, sir, we believe so !”
“Why, what makes you think so ? ” The flush
had gone out of the old man’s face, and a curious,
anxious expression replaced the things-go-very-
well-with-me aspect it had before worn.
“Did you know him, sir?” replied the boy,
with a degree of caution.
“ Yes, yes, / know him ! Of course—knew him
very well indeed. Don’t keep me in suspense,
boy, tell me ! ” .
“ I don’t think we ought to tell you, sir,” said
the second lad, even more discreet than his com-
panion, “ anything beyond the simple fact that we
believe he is dying.”
“Why, why, what is there beyond it? What
do you mean by saying it was awful—why can’t
you tell me?"
“Well, sir—we don’t know you, aiM what we
were speaking of we had heard in our homes. Our
fathers know the Colonel and each other ”
“ My lads, I know the Colonel too, better than
anybody does in this country. Nobody can know
him better—or owe him more than I do.” He
grew more excited as he spoke. “Look here, I’ll
give you half-a-sovereign apiece if you will tell
me—l must know, for the Colonel—one time
saved my life.” He stopped and shuddered, and
the moisture that wells from the spring of horrible
memories was as dew upon his forehead. “Yes,
yes, he saved my life ! Oh, tell me, is that good
man dying—and how ? ”
Not for the sake of the half-sovereigns (though
they were forthcoming at a later hour), but because
of the old gentleman’s evident distress and
anxiety, the youths repeated substantially what
they had heard ; and to their shocked surprise the
old man threw himself, or rather fell, reckless of
the neat purity of his attire, on the ground,
“ Oh, then, it has come true, then—it has come
true ! ”
It was the boys’ turn now to inquire :
“What has come true, sir?”
The only answer he could make, however, for
some minutes, and then only in muffled tones, for
his head was bowed upon his hands, was—“ Forty
year ago he said it, and forty year ago- .”
Then, he rose seemingly more aged by ten years
than he was when the boys first saw him.
* * *
Nothing more could the boys then learn
from him, but he desired them to introduce him
to their fathers.
“Major Hokes, of the ‘Kew and Hawthorne ’ —
I know him !” he said, when he had learnt the
name of the second lad. “Do me the favour,
boys, to go with me at once,” and the lads
returned homewards with this strange companion,
who muttered to himself distractedly as he went.
“Hokes, of the Kew and Hawthorn,” and the
other boy’s father, were encountered, and the three
seniors almost at once drew away, in earnest con-
versation, from the youths. The boys could not
make it out at all, and their wonderment became
the deeper when they heard their respective
parents make an appointment with the old gentle-
man for that afternoon in order to accompany him
to the dying Colonel’s residence.
* * #
Dying? They found him—dead. That last
shaft of light had pierced to the brain, and a brief
space only of unutterable, fathomless agony had
intervened after the withdrawal of the officers
before the Colonel-Commandant had ceased to
breathe.
The. mourning household at first refused to
grant permission to the old man to see all that
remained of the dead soldier. Had it not been,
indeed, for the earnest solicitations of the two
officers, personal friends of the Colonel, he would
have been refused.
But, at last, the ladies consented, and drawing
hard breaths and stumbling with an almost
palsied gait, the singular visitant was shown into
the room where the corpse lay. The officers ac-
companied him and moved, a few inches,
reverently, the polished-oak lid aside. The man
bent down. With great tears rolling down his
cheeks he kissed the sunken eyelids which were
(in their dreadful sightlestness) through
the orifices of the mask. So fearful had been the
death, so awful the ravages of the torture of his
later life, that the dead man’s face was not to
know in his coffin that sweet perfection of colour
which leaves so many faces in the cradle to bloom
once again in the coffin. There was no “last
look ” of stately, refined features to be enshrined
by his dear ones in the sacred chambers of reminis-
cence. They dare not remember the last!
The old man stooped, we say, to kiss the eye-
lids, and then, drawing himself up, he pulled from
an inner pocket a sheet of blue paper. It was a
half-sheet with faded lines of script running
across it, under a printed heading. He spoke
again.
Holding the paper in the gaze of the officers*—
“ Look, gentlemen ! This is what that man lying
there won for me. Read it! ”
One, scarcely knowing what he did, read it to
the other as follows :
H.B M.’s Penal Establishment,
Norfolk Island,
July, 183-
Dear Will,
You can have the life of Thomas Messenger,
per Lord Eldon, but understand that you must not look
upon this as a precedent.
Your affectionate father,
Maior-Oommandant.
“ That’s >vhat he got for me, gentlemen,
when he was a’boy, that’s what he got for me!
Fd have been hanged but for him, with all the
others in the great Mutiny, but he begged me off
because I was his mate—l was only a boy, you
see, and we used to go fishin’ to Philip Island
together. He made me promise to be a good man
when he saved my life —and I don’t think I’ve
broken my word. lam wealthy now, very rich,
but I’d give it all up for a touch of his warm,
livin’' hand ” He stopped and sobbed.
Then
“ Gentlemen, move the lid a little lower, please.
Let me place this in his hand—he won’t need any
passport to Heaven, but this might serve if he
did.”
The officers did as he requested. The gaunt
boniness of one hand, the left, showed through
the pipe-clayed glove. The other, too, was gloved,
but it was shapeless. The man slipped the paper
under the right hand.
“It was the right hand,” he said, “which
Whelan held when .”
“ When ?”
“When the boy—this great man that lies here
—brought me that paper to the condemned cell
where I was lying chained to Flash Whelan.
Whelan, who had led the rebellion, took hold of
his hand with his. “Give me your hand, Master
—’ter think as yer saved little Tommy’s life,
’ere! ” Master Will gave the wretch his hand,
and when Whelan grasped it in his, he said,
changing his voice, “May yer flesh drop of yer
bit by bit, for a-savin’ of his life, an’ not mine !’ (
“ That was forty year ago—and the curse has
been fulfilled.”
PRICE WARUNG.
.Balzac :—“No one stays in the Govern-
ment offices, under Bureaucracy, but idlers,'iu>: :
capables, and fools.” ■
When in Darlinghurst, Frank Gardiner
told the yarn that, ere his party stuck-up the gold-
escort, it was arranged that every man should fire
in the air to frighten the occupants of the coach.
One of the gang, however, did not fire, and later
on, when they were riding through the bush, his
gun.went off accidentally, putting a bullet through
Gardiner’s leg, after which little episode Gardiner
always made the man ride in front. The fellow’s
idea in retaining his fire was, no doubt, to show
in the event of capture, that he had not fired a
shot.
A number of Ontario (Canada) ladies un-
dressed, publicly in the market-place, stripped
their bodies of their corsets, and threw the dis-
carded embracers on a funeral-pyre, yelling with
fervent frenzy of religious spasm—“We will die
as God made us.” How little .they regarded their
language ! If they died as they were made, they
would die as red' as cooked prawns, bald, tooth-
less,-and indescribable. A corset thrown in, or
left out, matters little, from one aspect. But
from another—
“ To stay, or not to stay ?” the problem shoals
The narrow one of Bodice versus Souls.

Was it Murder?
(SPECIAL “BULLETIN” TRANSLATION.)
It was a sensational case. The old mem-
bers of the bar down there still talk of the stir
the affair made through the district.
It was at Aix, in Provence, a year or two after
the Crimean war. An enormous crowd, worked
to a high pitch of expectation by the strange,
mysterious features of the trial, had taken posses-
sion of the hall of justice.
# # *
The counsel for the defendant was called
upon.
He rose from his seat, grave, majestic, with his
black robe and cap, prepared to let loose the flood
of his commonplaces, conscious of being the mark
of all those eyes, enjoying in anticipation, not an
acquittal—that mattered little to him—but his
success as a celebrated criminal advocate.
The prisoner quietly made a sign that she wished
to speak.
The advocate, with a gesture of annoyed con-
sent, and a few words murmured to his client in
a low voice, sat down, and buried his face again
in Ids papers and musty books, as if to avoid
hearing what—much against his will and advice —
she was about to say.
Tall, dark, her shapely form showing beneath
the long mourning dress, the lifted widow’s veil
displaying her melancholy eyes, with their dark
rings,' and her hollow cheeks, waxy pale from the
long confinement, she must once have been very
beautiful. But, to tell the truth, she encountered
little sympathy. Public opinion was even
definitely hostile to her—especially amongst tlie
women.
She spoke :
* * *
“ You have heard, gentlemen, the indict-
ment and the crime with which it charges me. I
do not deny it. The facts set forth in it are true,
perfectly true. You have heard the witnesses,
both those called to support the accusation—whose
evidence I do not dispute—and those whom the
gentleman defending me has chosen to call —much
against my wish, I assure you—to testify to my
character, which is not in question. You have
heard, too, what the public prosecutor has said to
establish my guilt and make more certain of my
conviction, I have nothing to say either against
the indictment, or against the witnesses for the
prosecution, or against the address of the public
prosecutor. The murder is a clear one, which,
from the moment of committing it, I have
neither sought to deny nor to extenuate. The
reason why 1 now interrupt my honourable counsel
—and I sincerely beg his pardon for doing so—
is that there is one thing which I fear. He may
seek to mislead your minds by representing my
crime in unreal colours, surrounded by circum-
stances foreign to the case, and perhaps by
endeavouring to suggest motives very different
from those which really actuated me.
“ Yes, I did kill Jean Reynier, my husband ! I
killed him with premeditation ! Why ? Because
I loved him.
# # #
“As a girl I was poor. My family,
respectable middle-class people in a humble posi-
tion, stinted themselves to give me the best edu-
cation that was open for a woman. When, on
entering womanhood, I was left alone by my
parents’ death, my education had made me strong
for the struggle—as strong as a girl could be at
20. I turned to teaching to earn my living. My
independence I never yielded, not even, like other
women, the day I took a husband—a husband,
not a master 1 I married because I loved that
man.
“ When I met him he was a non-commissioned
officer of dragoons, a fine man, young, without
much education, and of a very ordinary type of
intelligence. Intellectually—l say it without any
false modesty—he was my antithesis. But, cradled
in literature and art though I was, I had never
given way to those dreams of childish romance,
of fanciful loves—all the false, affected sentimen-
talism that usually fills the heads of young girls
and makes them expect their future husbands to
be languishing puppets only fit for fairy-tales. By
temperament I have always been virtuous. Ido
not say that boastfully; it is my nature—l would
never have yielded to a man who was not my hus-
band. But I wished for a husband who should be
physically a true male, and mentally a being
whose mind would submit to the ascendancy of
my own.
“I found such a man in Jean Reynier; I loved
him, and I married him.
“About a year after our marriage he left me to
go to the war; his regiment was ordered to the
Crimea.
* * #
“I pause here to say one thing. It has
nothing to do with the matter of the trial, and
the prosecution has said nothing about it; but it
may find its way into private comments on my
case. I speak of it with great reluctance, but I
must state, simply and without any thought of
self-praise, that I have always been faithful —in
the moral sense as well as the physical sense of the
word—to the man I had chosen for my husband.
After my marriage I never thought of another ‘
man, either when we were living together, or
during his absence, or after his return. That
return — ! Well, now I will tell the whole story.”
For an instant she paused, deeply moved.
There was a stir in the crowd, still far from sym-
pathetic, while a momentary smile, half sceptical,
passed along the bench in front, where sat the
twelve jurymen. The Judge was impassive; he
might have been asleep. Below the accused the
celebrated advocate, his elbows on the desk, his
chin resting on his thumbs, shrugged his shoulders
and thought of his fine periods ruined. What did
she want to speak for? Where would she land
herself with all this futile talk ? God knew what
folly she was going to commit next! Clearly the
woman was mad ; and directly, if she would let
him speak at all he was very much inclined to
plead insanity.
She went on:
* * *
“In a cavalry charge at the battle of
Alma lie had both legs shattered by a shell, and
was left for dead amongst a heap of slain.
“As they were on the point of burying him
with the others, the surgeon-major, who was
examining each body before it was laid in the
great trench dug for the purpose, fancied he de-
tected some signs of life in the non-commissioned
officer of dragoons, and ordered him to be carried
to the hospital. There it was found necessary to
amputate both legs. The operation was success-
ful, and Jean Reynier, alas ! did not die. He re-
turned to France with a medal and a little pen-
sion, We resumed our life together. I thought
that love, the sentiment of duty, of natural pity,
would enable me to live with the unfortunate man
and alleviate, as far as possible, his lot.
“ But the ex-dragoon, formerly so proud of his
masculine beauty and his superb horsemanship,
had now but one thought, bitter and full of brood-
ing despair—rthat he should have become so
pitiable an object, inspiring a sort of horror; a
miserable cripple ! To be reduced to crawl about,
dragging painfully over the ground his mutilated
trunk, where once on review days, in his brilliant
uniform, mounted on a prancing steed, he had
been the admiration of the crowd !
“Neither my devotion, alas 1 nor my tenderness
could mitigate his horrible destiny. And I
thought that for him it would be sweeter to die.
“ I premeditated his death ; yes, gentlemen, in
that the prosecution are right. One night in bed ”
—the accused grew crimson and seemed to shiver
“in. the darkness, his breast, just where the
heart is, metjthe point of a knife, which sank in,
with a jet of warm blood that inundated me. I
rose shuddering with horror.
“My courage had not failed me; I had com-
mitted a murder that in my eyes was the accom-
plishment of a holy duty. But oh! that terrible
moment, when my hand moved under the pillow
in search of the liberating weapon, whose haft
was pressed against my right breast as I slew
him !
“ I was strong and resolute to strike, but it was
that my hand should not tremble, and that I
should not make him suffer ; for I loved him so !
“ Before the law, perhaps, it was murder. In
my eyes it was deliverance that I brought him.
In any case, my conscience acquits me. Without
fear, then, gentlemen, I await your verdict! ’
* # #
The twelve respectable citizens in the
jury-box did not see the matter in that light. The
prisoner died on the scaffold.
JULIEN MAUVRAC.
THEY ROLLED ON THE FLOOR.
On Monday, November 24th, 1890, the American
papers published the following news item :
“Mrs. Sarah S. Heusler, of No. 873, East One-
Hundred-and-Thirty-fourth-street, New York,
shot and killed herself yesterday morning. She
wasi a, lady of excellent character and high social
position, and a member of the Rev. Dr. Ramsay’s
Presbyterian Church. She was well-to-do and
very active in various public and private charities.
Since last July she had suffered fearfully from
indigestion and dyspepsia, which brought on
melancholia, and then a kind of insanity, under
the influence of which she took her own life.”
Her e is another stoiy, not so tragical, but with
the same moral. The narrator tells it of himself.
“Mostly,” he says, “we dread and fear death,-
yet once I prayed to die, and the reason was in
this wise. I pto Christmas, 1888, I had been a
healthy man, but at that time (a period of rejoicing
with so many) I felt depressed, languid, and tired.
My appetite left me, and I was much distressed
after eating the lightest food. My skin and eyes
became tinged with a dark yellow colour, and the
kidney secretion was like blood. The pain in my
stomach was almost unbearable and often lasted
from 12 to 14 hours without intermission. Some-
times I was in pain night and day, and was so bad
that my wife had to sit up with me through the
night. I was constantly sick, and troubled with
a stomach cough, and expectorated a quantity of
green phlegm.
“In spite of warm clothing and every comfort,
I was always chilled ; the cold shivers running
through me as if my blood were thin and poor. I
could take no solid food ; I 'lived on soups, milk,
puddings, &c., and after each meal I had empty,
gnawing, windy pains at the stomach, which
nothing relieved.
“ After a time an intolerable itching of the skin
spread all over me, as if my blood were poisoned.
Our family physician attended me for about a
year. Acting upon his advice I went to Harro-
gate, where I consulted another doctor, and drank
the waters, but feeling worse I returned home.
The bath attendant at Harrogate and others told
me I was suffering from blood poisoning, but this
the doctors never mentioned. The first doctor
said it was the passing of gallstones that gave me
such dreadful pain.
“ I now consulted an eminent specialist at Man-
chester, who confirmed what the other doctor had
told me, but none of them afforded me any relief.
“ In this miserable way I dragged on for six
months more, and became so much reduced I
could scarcely put one foot before the other, and
so thin that the rings fell off my lingers and rolled
on the floor. I was in such pain that I prayed to
die. and one of the doctors told a friend of mine I
could not recover.
“In August of last year (1890), whilst my suffer-
ings were at the worst, a book was sent to me by
post, telling of a medicine called Mother Seigel’s
•Curative Syrup. I determined to try it, and sent
to Mr. Evans, Chemist, Lymm, for a supply.
After taking the first bottle, I felt a little better,
and by persevering with the remedy I recovered
my appetite, and gradually gained strength. My
natural colour is now returned, and I feel as well
as I ever did in my life ; in fact, as well as I did
when a boy. I can eat any kind of food without
inconvenience, and have gained 30 pounds in
weight during the past three months. I may add
that previous to taking this medicine I was so
much altered that my friends, and even my pupils,
scarcely recognised me. I tell every one what
Seigel’s Syrup did for me. ”
The gentleman who makes the foregoing state-
ment is a person of position and known to all the
people of Lymm. He declines to permit the publi-
cation of his name, but the perfect truth of what
is here related is vouched for by Mr. J. H. Evans,
the chemist above named.
The case was an aggravated one of indigestion
and dyspepsia and its natural consequence. The
whole system had been poisoned and disordered by
the acids engendered by the fermentation in the
stomach, and, had not Seigel’s Syrup come to the
rescue just as it did a fatal result must have
followed in a brief time.

The Colonial Mutual Life Assurance
Society (Limited).
W have m our hands a small pamphlet relating
to the above society, which, in the first place,
attracts notice on account of its novelty. The
pamphlet consists of a series of extracts from
opinions of the British press relating to the last
Annual Report of the Colonial Mutual Life Assur-
ance Society, which have been typewritten and
then photo-lithographed. The issue has been
very neatly got up, but if one is attracted at first
by the novelty of the appearance of the pamphlet,
on looking into the contents, one’s attention is
still more attracted by the facts referred to in con-
nection with this progressive society.
The Financial Standard, of 20th June, 1891,
speaks as follows -
1 here are few, if any, offices that can show a
record of in ore rapid growth than the Colonial
Mutual. Fstablished in 1874, this office has risen, so
to speak, by leaps and bounds, and can now, in its
eighteenth year, show an account which would be
worthy of any of its century-old competitors.
What is perhaps one of the most pleasing
features in this office is that, notwithstanding the
continued increase of the business, the ratio of
expenditure to income shows a very considerable
reduction.”
I'rom the Insurance World and Monetary
Record, dated 24th June, 1891, the following
extract has been copied into the pamphlet :
1 he report presented at the recent meeting of
this prosperous and ably-handled Society, shows
no sign of slackening in the rate of progress, as
the new business of 1890 is in excess of that
recorded in any previous year, while a distinct
reduction has been effected in the expense ratio,
and a large addition made to the accumulated
funds, which now amount to upwards of
£1,200,000.
“ Notably good points in the report for 1890 are
toe reduction in the expense ratio, and the
splendid earnings of the invested funds. !>
Ihe above references give a glimmering of the
enormous size to which The Colonial Mutual Life
Assurance Society has grown in the comparatively
few years since it was started. On referring
to the report of proceedings at the last annual
meeting of the members, we find that the funds of
the Society exceed £1,200,000, and that at the
date of the meeting, the Policies in force in the*
Colonial Mutual amounted to over £10,000,000,
whilst the annual income at that date exceeded
£400,000. Figures like these make one wonder
how such an immense business could have
been accumulated in so short a time. We
do not know of any other Life Assur-
ance Society in the colonies of the United
Kingdom which has in the first 17 or 18 years of
its existence accumulated anything like so large a
business. This fact in itself is the strongest
possible evidence of the vigour with which the
business has been pushed, and the chairman’s
speech at the meeting referred to shows that the
vigour in pushing the business has not been at
the expense of permanence of character, for he
announced that, notwithstanding death claims and
discontinuances of all kinds, the Society had on
its books at that date no less than fiO per cent, of
the total amount of business that had been placed
on the books since the commencement. Those
who ate acquainted with the figures of Life
Assurance Offices generally, will know that such a
percentage is a most favourable one and speaks
volumes for the soundness of the business.
_ During the year 1890 the Policy Holders of The
Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society had
alloted to them the Bonuses resulting from the
profits made during the previous Quinquennium.
Ihe profits for this period were equivalent in
reversionary Bonuses to a sum exceeding £300,000,
being just about three times as much
as the Bonuses added to Policies at the
previous investigation. This, taken in con-
junction with the figures mentioned above,
will show at what a rapid rate the Society
is progressing. As our readers are aware, it is
the only Colonial Society that has extended its
business beyond the Australasian Colonies, and
one only has to read the Insurance Press of Great
Britain to see how favourably the Colonial Mutual
is regarded there, and what a satisfactory business
it is
Colonial Office to establish itself in the midst of
so many old-establ.shed British Life Offices, and
many may have thought that the step was too
bold a one, but the extension, like all others made
by the Colonial Mutual, has proved completely
successful, and the Society has been warmly wel-
comed by the offices in the Old Country for its
vigorous and straightforward way of doing busi-
ness and for the lines on which the Directors and
Management conduct it.

Knollys, smokes with the lighted end of the cigar
in his month ; and the Colonel states that no one,

As Long as Your Eyes are Blue.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Wilt tiiou love me, .sweet,-when my hair is grey
And my cheeks shall have lost their hue ? *
When the charms of youth shall have passed
away,
Will your love as of old prove true?
For the looks may change, and the heart may
range, i
And the love he no longer fond ;
Wilt thou love with truth in the years of youth
And away to the years beyond ?
Oh, I love you, sweet, for your locks of brown
And the blush on your cheek that lies—
But I love you most for the kindly heart
That I see in your sweet blue eyes—
-I'or the eyes are signs of the soul within,
Of the heart that is leal and true,
And mine own sweetheart, I shall love you still,
Just as long as your eyes are blue.
For the locks may bleach, and the cheeks of peach
May be reft of their golden hue ;
But mine own sweetheart, I shall love you still,
Just as long as your eyes are blue.
Sydney,
THE BANJO.
